###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MicroFocus COBOL ISAM files - are they C/ISAM compatible?

- **From:** "Tom Morrison" <t.morrison@liant.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T15:27:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ZBSG7.7101$dn3.1964256441@newssvr30.news.prodigy.com>`
- **References:** `<9savsh$hkg$1@news.netvision.net.il> <c8035ce7.0111081255.3897b337@posting.google.com> <b5b8d7c7.0111090154.71675d8e@posting.google.com>`

```

"Paulo Vieira" <pvieira@emporsoft.pt> wrote in message
news:b5b8d7c7.0111090154.71675d8e@posting.google.com...
> charles.godwin@ca.com (Charles Godwin) wrote in message
news:<c8035ce7.0111081255.3897b337@posting.google.com>...
> > My first question about an ODBC interface to a variable length record
> > is how do you map fields on the record to a database schema
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> for an 88 level.

Relativity is quite flexible in dealing with variable record lengths.

Multiple record types is a common reason for variable length records in a
file, and typically the best way is to use multiple table definitions for
the same physical file.  In this case, each record type usually has a fixed
length; the file is variable record length due to the intermixing of record
types.   Each table definition deals with only one record type, so the
variable record length aspect is not an issue.

There are other reasons for variable length records, however, and Relativity
has techniques to map these as well.

For example, one may have OCCURS ... DEPENDING ON  in the record definition,
making records of the same "type" with  a variable length.  Relativity
automatically normalizes OCCURS by generating multiple rows for the data
subordinate to the OCCURS clause, so in the case of OCCURS ... DEPENDING ON
the number of rows thus generated is different for each physical record
(depending on the DEPENDING ON variable, as it were).

There are other, more arcane ways to generate variable length records (eg
RECORD IS VARYING IN SIZE clause in the File Description entry).  To that
end, one may condition column values based upon the record length of each
record as it is read.  For each of the data items that might be "missing"
(due to a short record), one would define NULL condition based on the actual
record size, so that the column(s) based upon the potentially missing COBOL
data item(s) would return NULL.

Variable length records are only one of the difficulties faced while
providing ODBC access to indexed (and other) 3GL files.  Relativity is a
thorough solution which is available for Micro Focus COBOL on HP and which
has its developers reading comp.lang.cobol regularly -- and of course I
recommend Relativity!

Tom Morrison
Liant Software Corporation
www.liant.com
http://www.liant.com/products/relativity/

P.S.  Relativity is both the former and current name of Liant's ODBC access
product.  There was a short period in the product's history when it had a
different name, but that is behind us now.  :-)

Relativity is a registered trademark of Liant Software Corporation.

Relativity products contain software protected by:
U.S. Pat. No. 5,826,076
British Pat. No. GB2302430
Other Patents Pending
```

#### ↳ Re: MicroFocus COBOL ISAM files - are they C/ISAM compatible?

- **From:** Cygni.69@libero.it (Cygni.69)
- **Date:** 2001-11-15T11:10:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bebe2ef.22628789@news.interbusiness.it>`
- **References:** `<9savsh$hkg$1@news.netvision.net.il>`

```
On Wed, 7 Nov 2001 11:45:33 +0200, "Tzachi Nissim"
<tzachi@attunity.co.il> wrote:

>Hi,
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>so - which version of CISAM?

Tray this compiler directives on MF program
IDXFORMAT "n"      where n is
0	Default format for the operating system (same as 3 on Windows)
1	C-ISAM
3	Micro Focus default indexed file format
4	Optimized format for fast duplicate key handling
5	Btrieve with ANSI conformance
6	Btrieve without ANSI conformance
8	Large indexed file format

or alternativly 
FILETYPE "n" where n is
0	System-specific default (for this COBOL system, same as 3).
1	C-ISAM format.
2	Micro Focus Level II format.
3	Micro Focus COBOL format.
4	An optimized form of the format used by this COBOL system, for
fast duplicate key handling.

FILETYPE"integer" sets IDXFORMAT"integer" immediately.

If you can't rebuild your files or your project that produce you
files, try to convert them whit microfocus file converter,  
(I think you must convert them first in Line-Sequential and
subsequently in C-ISAM)  the tool is included in MF WORKBENCH or
NETExpress.

Hi!
```

- **From:** rtwolfe@flexus.com (Bob Wolfe)

"FRED FELTY" <ffelty@home.com> wrote:

>Express. I have been using the VB approach for the screens. Since none of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Fred:

Our COBOL sp2 GUI product has supported the ability to develop and
deploy Windows GUI screens for a COBOL application since Windows 3.0
in 1990.

We support all versions of Windows and our solution is compiler
independent.  That means tyou could develop a GUI for Fujitsu and run
the same screen handling code with NetExpress, or mbp Visla COBOL, or
CA-Realia COBOL, or ICOBOL, or IBM Visual Age COBOL, or Ryan McFarland
COBOL or Acucobol.

We support sophisticated screen controls such as treeview controls,
tab controls, embedded Active X controls and a host of other advanced
features.  We also allow you to run the same GUI screen definitions
and same source program as an Internet Thin Client application.  No
need to change the code.

We not only have Win32 GUI solutions, we also have web browser based
interface solutions and Windows Printing solutions as well.

Please send your questions via private e-mail.  I'll be happy to
assist you as well as provide you with an evaluation version for you
to try.




Bob Wolfe
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
When replying by e-mail, make sure that you correct the e-mail address.
Check out The Flexus COBOL Page at http://www.flexus.com
```

## Thread: COBOL for MVS — 8 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### COBOL for MVS

- **From:** "razzel" <razzel@eskimo.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-07T16:56:11-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9scl4j$uhp$1@eskinews.eskimo.com>`

```
I know COBOL 2000 has a SORT function for Tables in Working Storage but is
there any way to do this in COBOL for MVS.  I know you can read a table in
using a INPUT PROCEDURE, but when I try using an OUTPUT PROCEDURE it
requires a FILE defined in the FD.

Thanks for any Help
Russel Wheelwright
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL for MVS

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-07T19:13:15-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9scm7g$fq8$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9scl4j$uhp$1@eskinews.eskimo.com>`

```
No -
  There is no currently supported IBM mainframe COBOL compiler that has the
"table sort" feature defined in the draft of the 200x ANSI/ISO COBOL
Standard.

If this is an "important" feature for you, I suggest that you either use
SHARE to submit a requirement for IBM to implement this "sooner than
later" - or use the normal support channels to submit a REQUEST to do this.

Otherwise, you can wait until (*IF*) IBM implements the next COBOL Standard.

P.S.  If you use both an Input and an Output Procedure, you COULD do this
with a current COBOL compiler.  You would need to "loop" thru the input
table and move each table "element" (row) to an SD, then release it.  Then
in your Output procedure you would "build" your sorted table with each
returned item.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL for MVS

- **From:** "Brian Caddell" <briancad@netspace.net.au>
- **Date:** 2001-11-08T12:28:45+11:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9scn32$m9k$1@otis.netspace.net.au>`
- **References:** `<9scl4j$uhp$1@eskinews.eskimo.com> <9scm7g$fq8$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
Alternatively, you could read your rows into the internal table, and then
sort the rows in that table yourself, by comparing keys and swapping rows.
There are many algorithms for the sort process: single bubbles, double
bubbles, etc.

Brian Caddell

#### ↳ Re: COBOL for MVS

- **From:** Jerry Wilcox <Jerry.Wilcox@ucop.edu>

```
In article <9scl4j$uhp$1@eskinews.eskimo.com>, razzel
<razzel@eskimo.com> wrote:

>I know COBOL 2000 has a SORT function for Tables in Working Storage but is
>there any way to do this in COBOL for MVS.  I know you can read a table in
>using a INPUT PROCEDURE, but when I try using an OUTPUT PROCEDURE it
>requires a FILE defined in the FD.
>

No, there isn't a table sort in any MVS COBOL, however, your last
statement is a misunderstanding. There's nothing in an output procedure
that requires a file. I have many programs that sort and then my output
procedure does something other than write the sorted results to a file.

Given that, there's no reason you could not write an input procedure
that selected an item from your WS table and released it to the sort,
and then an output procedure that simply returned each sorted row and
inserted it back into your WS table.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL for MVS

- **From:** Colin Campbell <cmcampb@attglobal.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T21:22:04-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BECB97C.A9C51507@attglobal.net>`
- **References:** `<9scl4j$uhp$1@eskinews.eskimo.com>`

```
Why not write a little code that inserts the items into the table in the
proper sequence?  All it takes is a loop that moves the last item to last item
+ 1, then the next to last item to last item, etc., until the new item is
higher than the table item being tested.  If the input table is in sequence,
no moves ever take place, except adding the new item to the end of the table.

This can even be used when the table is "dynamic"; that is, not loaded
entirely at one time.

If items may be deleted from the table, another simple algorithm can be used
to delete item n, then move n+1 to n, item n+2 to n+1, etc., until the table
is completed.

In my experience, this works best with OCCURS ... DEPENDING.

If I were at work, I'd post the code, but you'll enjoy the challenge. <G>

> I know COBOL 2000 has a SORT function for Tables in Working Storage but is
> there any way to do this in COBOL for MVS.  I know you can read a table in
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Russel Wheelwright
```

of time and money in the future.

I had one contract at a site which had such a routine; IIRC, you passed it a
DD (or maybe DLBL, it was a while ago), a record length, key offset and
keylength, a destination address and length, and it did the rest.

I'm not suggesting this is easy, but it could be a great investment. (Except
that SORT will work on tables at some point in the future, rendering the
effort moot).

Just a thought...

MCM

### Dynamic allocations in COBOL

- **From:** COBOLMAN <member@mainframeforum.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T06:35:22-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bebcd8a$1_4@news.newzpig.com>`

```
Someone asked this at work and I have never heard of it so maybe the
comp.lang.cobol can help.

It is my understanding that there is a way to dynamically allocate files
from inside a Cobol program. For example, Lets say I have an input that
needs to be split into a varying number of outputs. Rather than having
hardcoded DD statements for every possible output file, the DDs can be
allocated dynamically from the program itself. On one day there may be
10 output DDs and the next may have 30 output DDs based on the input?

COBOL in use is COBOL II and COBOL for MVS

This message has also been posted on the Mainframe Forum
www.mainframeforum.com

#### ↳ Re: Dynamic allocations in COBOL

- **From:** scomstock@aol.com (S Comstock)
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T14:29:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20011109092948.23316.00001681@mb-ck.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<3bebcd8a$1_4@news.newzpig.com>`

```
COBOLMAN writes...

>Someone asked this at work and I have never heard of it so maybe the
>comp.lang.cobol can help.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well, yes and no. 

The general case is: yes, you can do dynamic allocation by calling a subroutine
that does SVC 99 type stuff; you were asking, I presume, about doing this
without calling a subroutine.

To do dynamic allocation directly in IBM mainframe COBOL requires COBOL for
OS/390, V2R2. Example:

.
.
.
          select persnnl assign to people.
.
.
.
 01  file-stuff.
      02  file-ptr      pointer.
      02  file-name.
            03                 pic x(12) value 'PEOPLE=DSN('.
            03  dsname   pic x(54) value spaces.
            03  disp         pic x(05) value ' SHR '.
      02  rc    pic s9(9) binary value 0.
.
.
.
 linkage section.
 01  in-name  pic x(54).
 procedure division using in-name.
      string in-name delimited by space
                  ')'        delimited by size into dsname
      set file-ptr to address of file-name
      call 'putenv' using by value file-ptr returning rc
      open input persnnl
.
.
.


If the file name is passed to this program from a calling program, and if at
run-time you do _NOT_ supply a dd statement named PEOPLE, then this code will
dynamically allocate the passed file name to the file name 'persnnl'.

<ad>
There are details not discussed in this short post. This, and a lot more, is
included in our two-day course "Beyond COBOL II", which brings COBOL
programmers up to date in the language facilities of COBOL from COBOL II V2R3
up through the latest release of COBOL for OS/390 & VM.

Details on this course can be found at
http://www.trainersfriend.com/d204descr.htm

</ad>


Kind regards,


Steve Comstock
Telephone: 303-393-8716
www.trainersfriend.com
email: steve@trainersfriend.com
256-B S. Monaco Parkway
Denver, CO 80224
USA
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Dynamic allocations in COBOL

- **From:** "Albert W. Paul" <alpaul@mindspring.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T10:20:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9sgs0r$rft$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3bebcd8a$1_4@news.newzpig.com> <9sgrt0$9fp$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
DYNAM

Dynamic Allocation Interface


INTRODUCTION
DYNAM provides an interface between a high level language such as COBOL or
PLI, and SVC 99, the system dynamic allocation  routine.  It allows the user
to acquire system resources as the need arises and free them when they are
no longer required.  DYNAM takes simple parameters consisting of character
strings and translates them into the text units required by SVC 99.  In
addition, a special information retrieval function can be called to provide
all the information available about a particular allocation.  This
information is returned in a fixed format data structure.  For more
information about SVC 99 refer to the IBM manual:

OS/VS2 MVS SYSTEM PROGRAMMING LIBRARY: JOB MANAGEMENT GC28-0627-2


WORK AREA
The first parameter to DYNAM is a work area.  This work area can be supplied
by the user or can be obtained for the user by DYNAM. If obtained by DYNAM
it can be used by subsequent calls to DYNAM and freed later, or can be
acquired and freed on the same call.  If the user supplies a work area, the
first word of the work area must contain the length in bytes.

 FORTRAN
  INTEGER WORK(500)/2000/
  CALL DYNAM (WORK, . . .)

 PLI
  DCL 1 WORK AREA,
  2 WA_LENGTH   FIXED BIN (31) INIT (2000),
  2 FILLER      CHAR (2000);
  CALL DYNAM (WORK, . . .)

 COBOL
  01  WORK.
         02  WORK-LEN  PIC S9(9) COMP SYNC VALUE +2000.
         02  WORK-AREA PIC X(2000).
  . . .
  CALL 'DYNAM' USING WORK . . .

If DYNAM is to supply its own work area, this can be done explicitly by
coding:

 DCL WORK    FIXED BIN(31) INIT(0);
 CALL DYNAM (WORK, 'INIT')

WORK then becomes a reference to the acquired work area and must be
specified on all subsequent calls to DYNAM as the first parameter.  The work
area can later be free by coding:

 CALL DYNAM (WORK, 'END')

If DYNAM is to acquire a work area to be used only during one invocation,
the work area is implicitly acquired by coding:

 CALL DYNAM (0, . . .)

This is used when the programmer only wants to perform one dynamic
allocation and then continue with his program.


OPERATIONS
The second parameter to DYNAM describes the operation you wish to perform.
There are two different  types of operations, those that have to do with
dynamic allocation and those that have to do with the interface itself.

 Interface Operations
 INIT  acquire a work area
 END  free the work area previously acquired by INIT

 Allocation Operations
 ALLOC  allocate a data set
 UNALLOC unallocate a data set
 CONCAT concatenate 2 or more DD names
 UNCONCAT unconcatenate previously concatenated DD names.
 REMOVE remove in-use attribute
 DDALLOC allocate a DD name
 INFO  retrieve information about an allocation


OPERANDS
The third and subsequent parameters supply operands needed to perform
dynamic allocation.  Only one operand parameter is required but it is
sometimes convenient to use a larger number, for example when obtaining
allocation parameters from a user at a terminal in an interactive manner.
Normal OS linkage conventions flag the last parameter in a parameter list so
a variable number of parameters is easily allowed if the high level host
language supports it.  Operand parameters must be delimited on the right by
a semicolon. Operands consist of a keyword and an optional value. if a value
is present it is separated from the keyword by an equals (=)  sign.
Operands are separated from each other by one of more spaces and an operand
string is delimited on the right by a semicolon.  If a keyword requires a
list of values, the values in the list are separated by a comma. Keywords
may be abbreviated by specifying enough of the keyword so that it is
unambiguous.  In cases where an entire keyword is  the same as the first few
letters of a longer keyword, ambiguity is resolved by picking the first
keyword.  In describing DYNAM keywords below the  unambiguous portion of the
keyword is written in uppercase.  An attempt has been made to minimize the
number of key words that require values.


ALLOC

This operation is equivalent to data set allocation during job step
initialization; the parameter list  to DYNAM is
 equivalent to a DD statement.   You can request most of the JCL services
that you can code on a DD statement.  In addition you can specify data set
passwords which do not have a JCL equivalent.  The following is a list of
JCL parameters and the equivalent DYNAM keyword.

 DD card parameter   DYNAM keyword

 COPIES=num    COPies=num
 DCB=(*.ddname)   DCBDD=ddname
 DCB=(dsname)    DCBDS=dsname
 DCB=(BLKSIZE=num)   BLKsize=num
 DCB=(BUFALN=key)   BUFAln=key  (key = D�F)
 DCB=(BUFIN=num)   BUFIN=num
 DCB=(BUFL=num)   BUFL=num
 DCB=(BUFMAX=num)   BUFMAX=num
 DCB=(BUFNO=num)   BUFNo=num
 DCB=(BUFOFF=num)   BUFOFf=num
 DCB=(BUFOUT=num)   BUFOUt=num
 DCB=(BUFRQ=num)   BUFRq=num
 DCB=(BUFSIZE=num)   BUFSize=num
 DCB=(BUFTEK=key)   BUFTEK=key  (key = A E R S)
 DCB=(CODE=key)   CODe=key  (key = A B C F I N T)
 DCB=(DEN=2)    D800
 DCB=(DEN=3)    D1600
 DCB=(DEN=4)    D6250
 DCB=(DIAGNS=TRACE)  TRAce
 DCB=(DSORG=key)   DSORG=key  (key = CX DA DAU GS
              PO PS PSU TQ
              TX TCAM VSAM)
 DCB=(EROPT=ABE)   ABE
 DCB=(EROPT=ACC)   ACC
 DCB=(EROPT=SKP)   SKp
 DCB=(KEYLEN=num)   KEYlen=num
 DCB=(LIMCT=num)   LImct=num
 DCB=(LRECL=num)   LRecl=num
 DCB=(MODE=key)   MODE=key  (key = C CO CR E EO ER)
 DCB=(NCP=num)   NCP=num
 DCB=(OPTCD=key)   OPTCD=key  (see note 1 below)
 DCB=(PRTSP=key)   PRTsp=key  (key = 0 1 2 3)
 DCB=(RECFM=key)   RECFM=key  (see note 2 below)
 DCB=(RECFM=F)   F
 DCB=(RECFM=FA)   FA
 DCB=(RECFM=FAS)   FAS
 DCB=(RECFM=FB)   FB
 DCB=(RECFM=FBA)   FBA
 DCB=(RECFM=FBAS)   FBAS
 DCB=(RECFM=FBM)   FBM
 DCB=(RECFM=FBS)   FBMS
 DCB=(RECFM=FM)   FM
 DCB=(RECFM=FMS)   FMS
 DCB=(RECFM=FS)   FS
 DCB=(RECFM=U)   U
 DCB=(RECFM=V)   V
 DCB=(RECFM=VA)   VA
 DCB=(RECFM=VAS)   VAS
 DCB=(RECFM=VB)   VB
 DCB=(RECFM=VBA)   VBA
 DCB=(RECFM=VBAS)   VBAS
 DCB=(RECFM=VBM)   VBM
 DCB=(RECFM=VBMS)   VBMS
 DCB=(RECFM=VBS)   VBS
 DCB=(RECFM=VM)   VM
 DCB=(RECFM=VMS)   VMS
 DCB=(RECFM=VS)   VS
 DCB=(STACK=num)   STACK=num
 DCB=(TRTCH=key)   TRTch=key  (key = C E ET T)
 DISP=(MOD)    MOD
 DISP=(NEW)    NEW
 DISP=(OLD)    OLD
 DISP=(SHR)    SHr
 DISP=(,CATLG)    CAtlg
 DISP=(,DELETE)   DElete
 DISP=(,KEEP)    KEEp
 DISP=(,UNCATLG)   UNCatlg
 DISP=(,,CATLG)   CCatlg
 DISP=(,,DELETE)   CDelete
 DISP=(,,KEEP)    CKeep
 DISP=(,,UNCATLG)   CUncatlg
 DSN=(name)    MEmber=name
 DSN=dsname    DSN=dsname
 DUMMY    DUMMY
 FCB=(name)    FORms=name
 FCB=(,ALIGN)    ALIgn
 FCB=(,VERIFY)    VERIFYF
 FREE=CLOSE    CLose
 HOLD=YES    Hold
 LABEL=(num)    DSSeq=num
 LABEL=(EXPDT=yyddd)   Expdt=yyddd
 LABEL=(RETPD=num)   RETpd=num
 LABEL=(,AL)    AL
 LABEL=(,AUL)    AUL
 LABEL=(,BLP)    BLP
 LABEL=(,LTM)    LTM
 LABEL=(,NL)    NL
 LABEL=(,NSL)    NSL
 LABEL=(,SL)    SL
 LABEL=(,SUL)    SUL
 LABEL=(,,IN)    Input
 LABEL=(,,NOPWREAD)   PASSWRite
 LABEL=(,,OUT)    OUTput
 LABEL=(,,PASSWORD)   PASSRead
 MSVGP=name    MSVGP=name
 OUTLIM=num    OUTLim=num
 QNAME=name    QNAME=name
 SPACE=(num)    BLOck=num
 SPACE=(CYL)    CYL
 SPACE=(TRK)    TRK
 SPACE=(,(num))    PRIMary=num
 SPACE=(,(,num))   SECondary=num
 SPACE=(,(,,num))   DIRectory=num
 SPACE=(,,RLSE)   RLse
 SPACE=(,,,ALX)    ALX
 SPACE=(,,,CONTIG)   CONtig
 SPACE=(,,,MXIG)   MXIG
 SPACE=(,,,,ROUND)   ROund
 SYSOUT=name    SYSOUt=name
 SYSOUT=(,name)   SYSOUProg=name
 SYSOUT=(,,name)   SYSOUForms=name
 TERM=TS    TErmfile
 UCS=(,FOLD)    FOLdmode
 UCS=(,,VERIFY)   VERIFYC
 UNIT=name    UNIT=name
 UNIT=(,num)    UNITCount=num
 UNIT=(,P)    PARallel
 VOL=(,,num)    VOLSeq=num
 VOL=(,,,num)    VOLCount=num
 VOL=(,,,,REF=name)   VOLRef=name
 VOL=(,,,,SER=(name))   VOLume=name
 VOL=(PRIVATE)   PRIVate

note 1:  For a complete listing of possible values for the  OPTCD parameter
refer  to the  IBM manual
OS/VS2 JCL.
note 2:  In  addition to the stand alone keywords for Fixed, Undefined,  and
Variable record formats others may be coded by using the RECFM=key key-word.
For a complete listing refer to the IBM manual OS/VS2 JCL.

 Others       DYNAM keyword
 DDNAME on DD card     DD=name
 PASSWORD      PASSWOrd=password
 /*ROUTE dest      REMOTE=dest
 assign the permanently allocated attribute to this resource PERManent
 assign the convertible attribute to this resource  CONVert

note:  For a complete explanation of the permanently allocated attribute and
the convertible attribute refer to SPL: JOB MANAGEMENT.


ALLOCR
This operation is the same as ALLOC except that information about the
allocation is to be returned to the caller.  The third parameter to DYNAM is
a data  area into which information is to be placed.  The fourth and
subsequent parameters are the same as the third and subsequent parameters
for the ALLOC operation.

The information data area has the following format:

 DCL 1 RETURN_AREA UNALIGNED,
      2 DDNAME  CHAR (8),
      2 DSNAME  CHAR (44),
      2 DSORG  BIT (16),
      2 VOLSER  CHAR (6);

 DDNAME, DSNAME, and VOLSER are self explanatory.
   DSORG is one of:
  x'0000' dsorg cannot be determined
  x'0004' TR
  x'0008' VSAM
  X'0020' TQ
  X'0040' TX
  X'0080'  GS
  X'0200'  PO
  X'0300'  POU
  X'0400'  MQ
  X'0800'  CQ
  X'1000'  CX
  X'2000'  DA
  X'2100'  DAU
  X'4000'  PS
  X'4100'  PSU
  X'8000'  IS
  X'8100'  ISU


UNALLOC
This operation unallocates a data set by DD name or data-set name.  The
following is a list of JCL parameters and the equivalent DYNAM keyword.

 DD card parameter     DYNAM keyword
 DISP=(,CATLG)      CAtlg
 DISP=(,DELETE)     DElete
 DISP=(,KEEP)      KEEp
 DISP=(,UNCATLG)     UNCatlg
 DSN=...(name)      MEmber=name
 DSN=dsname      DSN=dsname

 Others        DYNAM keyword
 DDNAME on DD card     DD=name
 change SYSOUT class     NEWClass=name
 put SYSOUT output into the hold queue   NEWHold
 take SYSOUT output out of the hold queue   NEWNohold
 change SYSOUT routing     NEWRemote=name
 unallocate the resource even if permanently allocated  UNAlloc
 remove the in-use attribute even if permanently allocated REMOVe


CONCAT AND UNCONCAT
These two operations concatenate and unconcatenate data-sets.  The data sets
can only be identified by using DD names of data sets currently allocated so
therefore the only key-word needed in the third parameter to DYNAM is
DD=name.  To concatenate you provide a list of DD names, e.g.
DD=SYSLIB,FILE2,FILE3.  The contention is then identified by the  first DD
name in the list.  To unconcatenate just provide DD=name.


INFO
This operation requests information retrieval.  The third parameter to DYNAM
is a data area into which information will be placed.  The fourth and
subsequent parameters are operands that specify the allocations about which
information is to be retrieved.

 The information data area has the following format:
 DCL 1 INFORMATION UNALIGNED,
    2 DDNAME  CHAR (8),
    2 DSNAME  CHAR (44),
    2 MEMBER  CHAR (8),
    2 STATUS  BIT (8),
    2 DISP  BIT (8),
    2 COND_DISP  BIT (8),
    2 DSORG  BIT (16),
    2 LIMIT  FIXED BIN (15),
    2 ATTRIBUTE  BIT (8),
    2 LAST_ENTRY BIT (8),
    2 TYPE  BIT (8);

DDNAME DSNAME and MEMBER are self explanatory.  If no member is allocated
then the contents of MEMBER will be unchanged.

STATUS, DISP and COND_DISP are the same respectively as those specified in
the DISP operand in JCL.

 STATUS is one of:
      X'01'  OLD
      X'02'  MOD
      X'04'  NEW
      X'08'  SHR

 DISP is one of:
      X'01'  UNCATLG
      X'02'  CATLG
      X'04'  DELETE
      X'08'  KEEP
      X'10'  PASS

 COND_DISP is one of:
      X'01'  UNCATLG
      X'02'  CATLG
      X'04'  DELETE
      X'08'  KEEP

 DSORG is one of:
      x'0000' dsorg cannot be determined
      x'0004' TR
      x'0008' VSAM
      X'0020' TQ
      X'0040' TX
      X'0080' GS
      X'0200' PO
      X'0300' POU
      X'0400' MQ
      X'0800' CQ
      X'1000' CX
      X'2000' DA
      X'2100' DAU
      X'4000' PS
      X'4100' PSU
      X'8000' IS
      X'8100' ISU

LIMIT is the number of allocations that must be freed before a new
allocation can be made.
 ATTRIBUTE is one of:
      X'80'  permanently concatenated attribute
      X'40'  in-use attribute
      X'20'  permanently allocated attribute
      X'10'  convertible attribute
      X'08'  dynamically allocated attribute

LAST_ENTRY indicates if this is the last entry in the list of allocations
for  this job.  It has the values:
      X'80'  if last entry
      X'00'  otherwise

TYPE is one of:
      X'80'  DUMMY data set
      X'40'  terminal
      X'00'  otherwise

The following DYNAM keywords are supported:
 Purpose     DYNAM keyword
 select an allocation by DD name  DD=name
 select an allocation by DSN name  DSN=name
 select an allocation by a relative number RELNO=num


EXCEPTIONAL CONDITIONS
DYNAM returns a condition code in registers 15 and 0 as well as in the
second word of the work area if one is provided.  The use of register 15
allows a COBOL program to interrogate the return code through the COBOL
special register RETURN-CODE and allows the same for PLI through the use of
the pseudo function PLIRETV.  The use of register 0 allows FORTRAN and
PASCAL to call DYNAM as a function that returns an integer result.  If the
actual allocation fails, then messages are written to the job log and the
return code from SVC 99 is returned to the user.  If DYNAM itself fails,
then the return code is a DYNAM return code.

SVC 99 Return Codes
 00 successful allocation
 04 environment, resource, or system routine failure
 08 request denied by installation validation routine
 12 invalid SVC 99 parameter list

DYNAM Return Codes
 16 invalid verb (parameter 2)
 20 invalid keyword (parameter 3)
 24 work area overflow
 28 invalid value keyword


LANGUAGE CONSIDERATIONS
In PLI remember to put in:
 DCL DYNAM  ENTRY OPTIONS(ASM,RETCODE);
 FETCH DYNAM;  /* JUST FETCH DYNAM ONCE */

In FORTRAN and PASCAL remember to link edit DYNAM into the load module you
wish to produce.  This is done by adding SYS1.LINKUMW to the SYSLIB data
sets at link edit or loader time.


EXAMPLES

1) In PLI allocate a new data set:
 DCL WORK    FIXED BIN(31) INIT(0);
 DCL PLIRETV BUILTIN;
 DCL DYNAM   ENTRY OPTIONS (ASSEMBLER,RETCODE);
 FETCH DYNAM;
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'INIT ');
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'ALLOC ', 'DSN=USERID.DATA NEW CATLG;',
  'VOL=USER01;',
  'TRK PRIMARY=1 SECONDARY=1;',
  'LRECL=80 BLKSIZE=6080 FB;')
 IF PLIRETV = 0 THEN
     code to execute upon success of allocation
   ELSE
     code to execute upon failure of allocation

2) In COBOL allocate an existing data set to ddname SYSIN:
 01  DY-STUFF.
      02  DY-WORK PIC S9(9) COMP SYNC VALUE +0.
      02  DY-WORK PIC X(5) VALUE 'INIT '.
      02  DY-ALLOC PIC X(6) VALUE 'ALLOC '.
      02  DY-TEXT  PIC X(100).
      . . .
 PROCEDURE DIVISION.
      CALL 'DYNAM' USING DY-WORK DY-INIT.
      . . .
      MOVE 'DSN=A0011.AA.T999D DD=SYSIN SHR;' TO DY-TEXT.
      CALL 'DYNAM' USING DY-WORK DY-ALLOC DY-TEXT.
      IF RETURN-CODE = ZERO THEN
          whatever
        ELSE
          do something to recover gracefully.

3) In PLI allocate a concatenation:
 . . .
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'INIT ');
 . . .
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'ALLOC ', 'DSN=SYS1.USERLINK;', 'DD=SYSLIB SHR;');
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'ALLOC ', 'DSN=SYS1.LINKUMW;', 'DD=FILE2 SHR;');
 CALL DYNAM(WORK, 'CONCAT ', 'DD=SYSLIB,FILE2;');

4)  Other  sample programs are  available in a  PDS called
SYS4.DYNAM.SAMPLES.  The programs included are:   DYASM an assembler program
to test DYNAM
 DYLISTA an assembler program to list allocated data sets
 DYCOB  a COBOL program that allocates COBOL data sets
 DYPLI  a PLI program to test DYNAM
 DYFORT a FORTRAN program

#### ↳ Re: Dynamic allocations in COBOL

- **From:** Daniel Jacot<daniel.jacot@winterthur.ch>
- **Date:** 2001-11-09T16:49:24+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<oOTG7.19025$xS6.30740@www.newsranger.com>`

```
On 9 Nov 2001 06:35:22 -0600, in article <3bebcd8a$1_4@news.newzpig.com>,
COBOLMAN stated...
>
>Someone asked this at work and I have never heard of it so maybe the
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Do you like the TSO syntax? If you do, you may use BPXWDYN. It's an IBM 'Tools &
Toys' program which is  free and should reside in your SYS1.LINKLIB. If is is
not there, you can download it from the net. The program works in all OS/390
releases.

The 'Tools & Toys' homepage is at:
http://www.ibm.com/servers/eserver/zseries/zos/unix/bpxa1ty2.html
Download the program from:
ftp://ftp.software.ibm.com/s390/zos/tools/bpxwdyn/bpxwdyn.unload
The documenttion is  here:
ftp://ftp.software.ibm.com/s390/zos/tools/bpxwdyn/bpxwdyn.html

There are samples in C, REXX and PL/I, but it works with COBOL as well. Want a
small sample?

IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.                                       
PROGRAM-ID. DYNALLOC.                                        
DATA DIVISION.                                                 
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.                                      
01 BPXWDYN       PIC X(8) VALUE 'BPXWDYN'.                   
01 ALLOC-STRING.                                             
05 ALLOC-LENGTH PIC S9(4) BINARY VALUE 100.                
05 ALLOC-TEXT   PIC X(100) VALUE                           
'ALLOC DD(INFILE) DSN(''LEM.JCL.CNTL(JICOBT20)'') SHR'.  
PROCEDURE DIVISION.                                            
CALL BPXWDYN USING ALLOC-STRING                            
IF RETURN-CODE NOT = ZERO                                  
THEN DISPLAY 'ALLOC FAILED, RETURN-CODE WAS ' RETURN-CODE 
ELSE DISPLAY 'ALLOC OK'                                   
END-IF                                                     
GOBACK.                                                    
END PROGRAM DYNALLOC.                                          

-------------------------------------------------
With kind regards
Daniel Jacot
-------------------------------------------------
visit us at: http://www.winterthur.com
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

- **From:** "Donald Steier" <dsteier@csc.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T14:59:42-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3bec4a96_9@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```
I disagree with part of your assessment. While it is true that people in
this forum are biased towards COBOL, asking for their honest opinions does
not hurt.

The point about personal development is more important than your career is
also somewhat misstated but correct. Your personal development (i.e. keeping
up in the field of programming) is important for your career. Is there a
future in COBOL, for now yes there is. Will there always be is up for
speculation. Is it good to know it, yes but you should diversify your
skillset to other languages. BASIC, COBOL, C++ etc. are all languages that
you should learn and how they interact with database programs such as DB2,
Oracle and Access. At some point in your career you may end up being a
Database Administrator, Systems Analyst or something else entirely. Think of
programming as a starting point, not an ending point. COBOL as a language is
still strong in the market, but limited to Mainframes for the most part. C++
and Java tend to be more cross platform and BASIC is just downright fun (and
still used for various applications in the market as well).

The person that wrote the response below is correct in that you must take
responsibility for your own decisions and actions. How you chart your future
depends upon the decisions you make. But it does not hurt to know COBOL
since it is still used.
```

Don:

Although I do agree with many of your comments, I must take exception
to your statement that COBOL is limited to the mainframe for the most
part.

COBOL has been available on the desktop platform and grown dramtically
on this platform for over 2 decades

It has thrived even longer in the UNIX world and continues to be used
extensively on UNIX server platforms.

I'm very well aware of this because the UNIX & PC markets have been
almost an exclusive focus for Flexus as well as a number of other
companies offering COBOL programming tools and compilers.

>and Java tend to be more cross platform and BASIC is just downright fun (and
>still used for various applications in the market as well).

I'm not sure that I agree that Java has better cross platform support
than COBOL.

I've not heard of an AS-400 version of Java (perhaps one DOES exist).
COBOL exists on this platform.

How about Java on an Open VMS platform?  I'm not sure if Compaq offers
a Java compiler on Open VMS.  They offer a COBOL compiler.

As long as the programmer adheres to the ANSI standard while writing
their source code, the program should remain quite portable.

>The person that wrote the response below is correct in that you must take
>responsibility for your own decisions and actions. How you chart your future
>depends upon the decisions you make. But it does not hurt to know COBOL
>since it is still used.

I agree with the above 100%.



Bob Wolfe

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-11-27T12:06:59-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OIPM7.6240$Ju6.1527858@news20.bellglobal.com>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3bec4a96_9@goliath.newsgroups.com> <h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <3C03F21E.CFE3C76F@Azonic.co.nz> <RiOM7.87$kY4.2054@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <GSOM7.6191$Ju6.1508523@news20.bellglobal.com> <9jPM7.89$kY4.1965@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com>`

```
"Donald Steier" <dsteier@csc.com> wrote in message
news:9jPM7.89$kY4.1965@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com...
>
> "Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote in message
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > And many business applications are written in it. Even before COBOL
was
> > > developed.
> > >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I believe that Basic was developed at Stanford, in the very late 60's or
early 70's.  That was what I was told when I taught a course on it, circa
1971.  At that time, it did not work on any of Dec's mini's, though it was
ported to the PDP-11 about that time.  It was running on the PDP-10 only
when I taught the course.  Cobol, at the time, was a well established
lamguage running on dozens of machines.

Basic did not really start to be used for bussiness until the mid to late
70's, when mini-computers came to the fore.  The the PDP-11 and the Nova
made basic their main language.  By that time, however, Cobol was 10 to 15
years old.  The BASICS used in that era were horrific for business, having
only floating point or integer math.  It was another ten years before there
was such a thing as a currency data type.

Donald
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T07:17:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C048F6C.E381ADE8@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3bec4a96_9@goliath.newsgroups.com> <h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <3C03F21E.CFE3C76F@Azonic.co.nz> <RiOM7.87$kY4.2054@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <GSOM7.6191$Ju6.1508523@news20.bellglobal.com> <9jPM7.89$kY4.1965@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com>`

```
Donald Steier wrote:
> 
> > > And many business applications are written in it. Even before COBOL was
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ironically, I thought the same thing when I heard that. But it is true.

Cobol was developed in 1959 for the writing of business applications.

BASIC was written by Kemeny and Kurtz in 1962 (note this is after 1959)
and was originally only a time-sharing teaching system with no mechanism
to write 'applications'.  The 'B' stands for Beginners. It wasn't until
the late 60s that DEC and HP made Basic (using incompatible dialects)
into a useful enough language for engineers and mathematicians.

Basic for business applications was probably mid-70s after adequate file
handling was available.
```

The primary use was - according to the language's inventors (but what do
*they* know, anyhow?) - *teaching*, not building systems.  Consider the
acronym: Beginner's All-purpose Symbolic Instruction Code.

Now, consider:  'The language found to be best-suited to filling our
business's needs for manipulating the words and numbers upon which we base
our survival and competitive edge is a Visually-oriented dialect of the
Beginner's All-purpose Symbolic Instruction Code.'

>Its quality of fun isn't a significant trait in my evaluation of what
>language is appropriate for a particular business need.  

Aye... the 'other hand' of this was shown me a while back; COBOL '74
couldn't deal with fractional exponents necessary for calculating the
yields of certain financial instruments so the subroutines to do so were
written in BASIC.

DD

That is not what I understood. Does Flow-matic ring a bell? But you are
correct in the timeline of programs, BASIC came later. But FORTRAN came
before COBOL according to the history records. But that was due to the fact
that electronic computers were not used for business purposes until IBM
started marketing computers for that purpose.

But we are arguing history. Kinda silly to get defensive over what has
happened. What will happen is more interesting. I suspect that the remaking
of programming languages for the Internet, Intranet and whatever else comes
after that will determine what form they ultimately take. Which begs back to
the original question of is there a future for COBOL? I think that we can
all agree that in the relatively near future there is.

pick a job or jobs that will allow you to get and stay diversified.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T08:01:51+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C0499EF.2FF489F7@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3bec4a96_9@goliath.newsgroups.com> <h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <3C03F21E.CFE3C76F@Azonic.co.nz> <RiOM7.87$kY4.2054@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com> <jvyyrzubevmbagrfvasbezngvpnpbz.gnhfv12.pminews@News.CIS.DFN.DE> <ynQM7.96$kY4.2327@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com>`

```
Donald Steier wrote:
> 
> That is not what I understood. Does Flow-matic ring a bell? 

'Flow-Matic' is one of the predecesors of Cobol, as are 'Commercial
Translator' and 'FACT'. These all contributed to the Cobol language of
1959.

> But you are correct in the timeline of programs, BASIC came later. 

So, 'even you' can admit being wrong   ;-)

> But FORTRAN came > before COBOL according to the history records. 

Fortran isn't BASIC.

> But that was due to the fact
> that electronic computers were not used for business purposes until IBM
> started marketing computers for that purpose.

Complete nonsence.  The first major use of computers for business
purposes were LEOs (Lyons Electronic Office) developed by English
Electric and operational in 1951.  They just had their 50 year
anniversary (see www.leo-computers.org.uk).

> But we are arguing history.

_We_ are arguing history, you are arguing half-remembered nonsense.

> Kinda silly to get defensive over what has happened. 

We are 'getting defensive' over what did not happen.

> What will happen is more interesting. I suspect that the remaking
> of programming languages for the Internet, Intranet and whatever else comes
> after that will determine what form they ultimately take. Which begs back to
> the original question of is there a future for COBOL? I think that we can
> all agree that in the relatively near future there is.

I do my internet programming in Cobol. Originally the internet was
programmed to do simple tasks and provide information.  To make the
sites 'active' various scripting langues such as Perl, PHP, JavaScript
were developed. Later internet programmers tried writing business
programs in the languages they were used to.  Many of these programmers
and languages do not meet the needs that business applications actually
require.

For the same reasons that Fortran and BASICs made poor business
languages, so do PHP and Perl.  Cobol is an excellent language for
writing business applications, so far there is no other language that is
as suited to this task (PL/I came close), not C, not C++, not Java, not
Python.

The main reason is that to use these languages the programmer must care
about the language, they must know how the language works.  To use Cobol
the programmer does not need to care about the language they need to
know how the _business_ works.

With Cobol programmers can write using their site subset without having
to know or care about such things as 'operator precedence', 'coersion',
'side effects' or any other mumbo-jumbo that gets between their business
knowledge and working programs.  In most cases 'computer scientists' do
care about such things but get bored senseless if you mention 'credit
note' or 'statement of account'.

Cobol will remain the main business language because Cobol programmers
are the ones who will write business applications.  C++, Java, and Perl
programmers will wander off and do something more interesting (to them)
such as learning yet another new language.
```

```


"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

> Interested in your reference to LEO and CRAM, Jimmy.
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

As the Doc would say, "My memory is porous....". I don't actually have a mental
picture of the Leo. The CRAM - vaguely, I *think* the cards were 'overhead' and
dropped down into a 'reader' ????

The main reason for visiting was the Lector - to parallel their bakery van
selling operation. They established daily routes for each van salesman, and
printed out Lector documents in route sequence. Plain paper, as I recall, and
they printed everything, descriptions and even the boxes to 'bar-mark'. A
two-part document (1) Customer and (2) for EDP. There was document 'waste' but
marginal, considering how early the introduction was.

The bit that intrigued us most was the marketing approach. On the left hand-side
were shown the sales in units for the past four deliveries. Retailer says, "No,
I don't think I need cup cakes, this time". "Ahh!",  replied the salesman, "You
had  2 dozen four weeks ago, then 3 dozen, and one dozen for the past two
weeks.......". "Oh well maybe I should take some....".

Although I shared space with our analyst who was covering van selling my
concentration was on the "advance" sales -  cases, and trucks of whey, curds,
milk powders etc. We did parallel their operation for van sales, pre-print order
delivery documents  per route but keypunched. (Couldn't have done it with
"advanced sales"; under certain circumstances we had a 7-part order/delivery
document - particularly when goods were shipped either by BR or the Unilever
transport subsidiary SPD Ltd. That baby sure took some thought in designing, not
showing the cost price on EVERY copy and carbon-backing the areas where we
wanted the hand-writing to duplicate through to under-copies). A thought - maybe
I should set up a test project for those gentlemen who so quickly refer to
themselves as analyst/programmers <G>

As you know, instead of CRAM we used RCA's MCF (originally designed for USN for
gunnery computations). That consisted of eight magazines and the magnetic cards
were programmed to be extracted from a magazine and then 'sucked' down a tramway
where they did a figure-of-eight around the Read/Write head, then they were
'sucked' back into the appropriate magazine. Eventually failed technically 'cos
some of the cards got mangled up on the tramway, when leaving 'home' or
returning to 'base'.

Didn't come up against ICL's OMR - UDT (Universal Document Transport) until I
made it to Debenhams in '67 and we successfully used it for Payroll and Food
Accounts Payable.

Can you believe it - Debenhams directors were very much third generation male
fuddy-duddies in my time. See now from the international copy of the Daily
Express that the head honcho is now a lady !

Alas. Unigate sells not a drop of milk any longer.

PS: A closing thought. We haven't had a peep out of Tony Dilwoth in yonks. Do
you think it's you two Kiwis who pissed him off <G> Mind you, a few of our
American cousins contributed as well !

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T13:37:16+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3bec4a96_9@goliath.newsgroups.com> <h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com>`

```
Donald Steier <dsteier@csc.com> wrote in message
news:h2yM7.83$kY4.2211@dfw-service2.ext.raytheon.com...
> I disagree with part of your assessment. While it is true that people in
> this forum are biased towards COBOL, asking for their honest opinions does
> not hurt.
>
No, it doesn't. Neither does asking the opinion of your next door neighbour
or the guy who sells you a newspaper every day. If you want opinions there
are any number of places you can get them.

The point I was gently making is that asking for an opinion you know to be
biased is just as useless as asking for an opinion you know to be
uninformed.

Unbiased informed opinion is the best kind, but even then you should only
take it into consideration; the final decision  (for things which affect
YOUR life, like jobs, etc.) must be your own.


> The point about personal development is more important than your career is
> also somewhat misstated but correct. Your personal development (i.e.
keeping
> up in the field of programming) is important for your career.

"somewhat misstated" ?  "...but correct" ?!   How is that possible?

If something is misstated, it cannot be correct, by definition. (The prefix
"mis" indicates incorrectness). If it is correct, then it cannot have been
misstated. Think about it...

I was puzzled by your take on this but then I read on and I saw that your
interpretation of "personal development" is "keeping up in the field of
programming"...

So now it is all clear. Your idea of "development" is increasing your
programming skill.

That is NOT what I meant when I said "your personal development is more
important than your career". I meant the development of the individual as a
person. The ability to make decisions, take responsibility for them, ...grow
as a person. I believe this is far mor important than any career move.

The question of the Future of COBOL has been discussed in this forum
countless times. The first few times (about 5 years ago) it was marginally
interesting. Now it is just boring. Search the archives if you want to see
what has been said about it.

Whatever is said here about it won't change it.

Yes, we could agree to have a continous running thread on this subject and
those of us who are bored rigid by it simply don't have to contribute.
(Actually, that is kind of what is happening...<G>)

While I may not be interested in discussing the future of COBOL any more
(everything I have ever wished to say about that is on record...) I DO have
some concern for the welfare of students and that is why my response was
couched the way it was.

Having read the thread I see that you have been disabused of some of your
notions regarding COBOL  so I guess there is some value in it after
all...<G>

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-29T12:14:29+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

Michael Mattias <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote in message
news:vY4N7.50$XV4.14128@dfiatx1-snr1.gtei.net...
> Peter E. C. Dashwood <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:3c0452f1_10@Usenet.com...
> >

when you ask them their opinion about something, they give you many (if not
all) of the alternatives available. But everyone is biased in one way or
another. It then depends upon the persons honesty. And if they make an
attempt to be objective (which no one can be 100% of the time), then they
will give an opinion much like the one I gave before. COBOL is good to know,

Making decisions and taking responsibility for them has a name.  It is
called life.  When we stop making those decisions, it is called death. We
all make our decisons based upon the best information that we have available
when it comes to life. I simply interpreted your statement in the context of
the forum instead of the more general terms of life. Our environment,
education, social background and an entire host of external and internal
factors make up what is personal development. My apologies for
misunderstanding your statement, but my answer applies to the persons
question as well as to your response (which as I said I took in context to
the forum and the original question).

Why one person becomes a programmer, another the head of a corporation and
still another a thief is for another forum. Not that I disagree with your
statement now that you have clarified it for me (thank you very much). The
person that asked the question, and indeed all of  us, need to be
responsible for our decisions and actions. But I disagree with your
statement that it is more important than any career move. Because part of
how you grow and develop in this world can be strongly influenced by your
career choice. Being a  buyer for a corporation, you may get the opportunity
to travel the world to test new products. Being a drug dealer, you get to
experience people trying to kill you and being arrested and put on trial.

Life itself is a learning process. We just have to be smart enough to look
at what happened and say "Gee I don't think I'll do that anymore". Or that
worked great, lets do it again. Life is all different shades of grey. What
works for one person does necessarily not work for another. And I think on
that point we can both agree.

>cobol world, but my friends say that there isnt any future as a COBOL

Hello, Joske

That depends on a number of factors - 

Questions for yourself - what are your goals ? Do you *want" to work
in COBOL? If yes, where in the world do you intend to work - I seem to
recall that France preferred PL1 to COBOL, so there may be less of it
there, but in North America the odds are better - you have to do some
research. Also, mastery of a second computer language may help - think
about conversions, for example.

Questions for your friends - Have they ever worked in COBOL? And the
last, but the most important question, for them is : on what facts do
they base their statement?

Since you came to this newsgroup for another opinion, it seems that
you are putting your friends' opinion in doubt - why not explore that
doubt with them - find out on what your doubt is based.

Either way, let us know how your search for a career turns out; people
here give advice, many, I'm sure, would like to know how useful that
advice was.

Robaire
```

#### ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

- **From:** "Edwin Earl Ross" <EdwinEarl@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-11T20:22:44+00:00

```
Decide where you want to live.

Read the classifieds for that city and find out what companies are offering
jobs (by language, operating system, platform, whatever is important to
you). Learn whatever language you need to work where you want to work. Apply
for the job of your dreams and others.

If city isn't an important factor, use whatever selection criteria you think
is important.

There are still many jobs in COBOL, and new code being developed all the
time.

Try to stay ahead of the technology guessing game by learning several
languages well. In thirty years, I've coded in as many languages, including
Assembler, Fortran, Pascal, C, and COBOL. So far, there has always been more
work in COBOL than any other language, but things are changing.

#### ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

- **From:** "Ian Stewart" <ian@incognito.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-13T20:32:54+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<D04I7.2298$Eq.245950@news.xtra.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be>`

```
I was just about to unsubscribe this group which I haven't looked at for
months.  Thought I'd jump in and offer my $0.02 ...

My background is IBM AS/400, and I've been involved with one site that was
running a COBOL application.  Most of my other work has been RPG, also on
AS/400.

I'm perceiving a drop in the amount of work available for both of these
skills, and have been actively getting involved with Java and internet
skills over the last year (locally, i.e. South Island NZ, however, following
newsgroups and postings for jobs internationally this would appear to be a
more general trend).

Personally, I wouldn't be taking on COBOL at this stage.  YMMV depending on
your situation and requirements, and what's on offer locally of course, but
I suspect it'll be a relatively static or slowly declining market (granted
from a large installed base so it will be around for quite a while
irrespective of what's currently flavour of the week ...)  You probably
wouldn't be competing against too many young graduates looking for their
first job though, I doubt have COBOL very far up their list of preferences!

The internet and related stuff is likely to be a rollercoaster ride by
comparison (but probably a lot more opportunities and I'd say it's hardly
started yet) ... and then there's other skill sets (C/C++ etc) to consider
...

On the whole, I'd suggest that picking a "language" only, and expecting that
to carry you very far by itself is no longer going to cut it.  You probably
need a range of technical skills, which is going to be a constantly moving
target.  (If you do decide to learn COBOL, by all means get a handle on SQL,
XML, HTML, HTTP, Linux, perhaps Java or C++ ... even if you have to do some
of it in your own time!  The resources to learn and experiment it can be
found more or less for free on the web ... it's bound to pay off somewhere
sooner or later - probably sooner!)

Good luck ...

-Ian.
```

#### ↳ Re: cobol, is there a future?

- **From:** tmartin9@caramail.com (Timothy Martin)
- **Date:** 2001-11-18T13:04:26-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bf43e12d.0111181304.602e2bd7@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<01XG7.14820$XM4.671@afrodite.telenet-ops.be>`

```
"Joske Vermeulen" <adil_polat@hotmail.com> wrote 

> i'm a junior analyst-programmer, and i'm concidderring to step in to the
> cobol world, but my friends say that there isnt any future as a COBOL
> programmer?
> so what do you think

I am currently working on a very large COBOL project in Europe.
Organization "ABC" is adding functionality to their system in
order so that organization "XYZ" will be able use the same system
as well.  After this project is done, there will be another 
smaller project to add the functionality needed by a third 
company, organization "MNO".  And then after that they are going 
to start to look for other customers as well.

All three of these organizations are very large, and our product
will be running at all three.  Maybe in another 2 or 3 years
they will start working on a Windows type, non-CICS, version,
but to start things off it is all mainframe.  In the begining, the
mainframe version will be the bread-and-butter version.  Years
later the DB2 databases on the mainframe will probably still be
the back-end to the system.  

I can look at what we are doing and feel, that at least in this 
case, there is a future for COBOL running on mainframes.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol & PL/1

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-10T23:44:30+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bed066f_7@goliath.newsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1s_G7.115814$B72.30976955@news1.rdc1.az.home.com>`

```
It is indeed unusual to find them both on the same site...

Still, there is no reason why they shouldn't be used together and the fact
that they are usually not is probably more historical than practical.

PL/1 was originally PL/I (Programming Language International) but IBM
revised it when they realised it was not enjoying the universal uptake they
had hoped it would. Later versions of the documentation show PL/1 indicating
it was the first attempt at an International Programming Language. (Some of
us are still waiting for PL/2...<G>)

As people are starting to realise that it makes sense to use the best
Language for a particular purpose, and as PL/1 is a "programmer's language"
designed around the principles of structured programming, it will not be so
surprising to find mainframe sites using PL/1, in conjunction with COBOL,
for tasks that might previously have been done in Assembler.

I have only used PL/1 with mainframes and only for subprograms, but I found
it effective and useful. The main "advantage" it has over COBOL is that it
can implement dynamic arrays. In my day this was done without resorting to
GETMAIN at the programmer level (the dynamic run time calls for memory were
indeed implemented by calls to GETMAIN, but no assembler knowledge was
required and it was transparent to the programmer). Nowadays of course, with
the LE environment, God knows how they implement it, but it apparently
works....

(Of course, COBOL has many other features which make it useful and in which
PL/1 is not so strong...they are both useful.)

As for things to avoid in PL/1 programming, avoid the same things you would
avoid in ANY programming... <G>

Thanks for letting us know at least one site has this environment.

Pete.

"Rufio" <davecawdell@home.com> wrote in message
news:1s_G7.115814$B72.30976955@news1.rdc1.az.home.com...

> all, or am I likely to just find them in different steps in the JCL?

West (now part of Qwest) has applications written in COBOL, PL/I, Assembler, C,
and other strange and mysterious languages.

This is why our LE courses, our DB2 courses, and many of our CICS courses are
multi-lingual, so the students specify which of these languages to use in
lecture, (and it's often more than one), and each student can run the labs in
the [programming] language of his or her choice.

Both PL/I and COBOL have gone through enormous changes in the last several
years. And in the LE environment they work together very well.

You'll find many shops that are mostly PL/I with little or no COBOL in Europe

"S Comstock" <scomstock@aol.com> wrote in message

OK, I'll qualify what I said...<G>

In my experience (mainly outside the USA, in Europe and Australasia) it is
unusual to find PL/1 and COBOL on the same site. However, I agree that more
sites are using more languages.

(I have always advocated usng the best tools for the job.)

> This is why our LE courses, our DB2 courses, and many of our CICS courses

Good. That is a sensible approach.
>

fact

they
> >had hoped it would. Later versions of the documentation show PL/1
indicating
> >it was the first attempt at an International Programming Language. (Some
of
> >us are still waiting for PL/2...<G>)

>>>>>>>>>
> That's funny. I always thought it was a capital 'I' as in 'Roman Numeral
One'.
> PL/I was to be the one language anyone ever needed. At least that's what
they
> told us in going through the IBM training to be a Systems Engineer back in
'68.
> [Or at least that's what my feeble memory thinks I heard.]
>

PL/I was released in 1964 and found little uptake outside of IBM. COBOL 61
was well established and had built on the success of COBOL 59.
Most commercial shops were happy to use COBOL. In those days it was a very
brave EDP manager who would go up against the dictates of IBM so some shops
did try the new language. Those that didn't, usually pleaded an existing
investment in COBOL that made it non-viable.

IBM decided to re-market the product and re-badged it as PL/1. (And yes,
they did say that the "I" was a Roman numeral "1". This saved re-printing a
lot of existing material. However, if you look at the manuals released when
the product was standardised in 1976 (ANS), you will see that the "I" has
become a "1". (A capital "I" is not normally used to denote a Roman "1"
anyway; it should be a small "i")

I don't doubt that if you were on an internal IBM course in 1968 you would
have been given the official "party line" and to this day that is what many
people "remember". Certainly, that is how IBM "remember" it...I worked for
them at Hursley Park for a while and there was only one other guy there who
knew what I was talking about, when the subject came up over lunch one day.

BY 1972 when PL/M was released, a number of shops were using PL/1 and by
1976 when it attained ANS standard there was a fair penetration of the
available market.  (PL/M disappeared without trace, as the market could not
really sustain TWO contenders for COBOL's crown.) However, it always had to
compete with COBOL and that is why there was a "division" between shops that
used COBOL and shops that used PL/1. It really had little to do with the
relative merits of either language; more to do with IBM pressure and
marketing.

> >
> >As people are starting to realise that it makes sense to use the best
> >Language for a particular purpose, and as PL/1 is a "programmer's
language"
> >designed around the principles of structured programming, it will not be
so
> >surprising to find mainframe sites using PL/1, in conjunction with COBOL,
> >for tasks that might previously have been done in Assembler.
> >
> >I have only used PL/1 with mainframes and only for subprograms, but I
found
> >it effective and useful. The main "advantage" it has over COBOL is that
it
> >can implement dynamic arrays.
>
> Well, and these: bit manipulation (until the COBOL 2002 standard is
approved),
> handling of extended precision floating point data, handling complex
numbers,
> condition handling [although both languages can now use LE condition
handling,
> PL/I can do this in native PL/I, with no calls to LE services], the use of
> controlled storage, and a few other features, I'm sure.
>
> >In my day this was done without resorting to
> >GETMAIN at the programmer level (the dynamic run time calls for memory
were
> >indeed implemented by calls to GETMAIN, but no assembler knowledge was
> >required and it was transparent to the programmer). Nowadays of course,
with
> >the LE environment, God knows how they implement it, but it apparently
> >works....
> >
> >(Of course, COBOL has many other features which make it useful and in
which
> >PL/1 is not so strong...they are both useful.)
>
>

> Can you site any examples of a COBOL feature not available in PL/I? I'm
not
> saying there aren't any, just like to make your statement concrete.
>

Why? I'm not looking for an argument at the moment (the sun is shining
here...<G>) Please re-read what I said and don't try and push "not so
strong" into "not available"... Self documentation, REDEFINES, and file I/O
are all strengths of COBOL which are "not so strong" in other languages.

I think I already covered that both languages are useful so I don't see the
point of pursuing this.

> Both PL/I and COBOL have gone through enormous changes in the last several
> years. And in the LE environment they work together very well.
>
> You'll find many shops that are mostly PL/I with little or no COBOL in
Europe
> and Asia. PL/I was [is?] much more successful abroad than in the US.
>

Yes, you will. It is a hangover form what I outlined above. People went
EITHER PL/1 or COBOL.

Fortunately, this old perception is changing and, as Source code becomes
more and more irrelevant in the near future, it will change further.

Pete.

"SkippyPB" <swiegand@nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message
news:0F8282FFFD147787.0CB23FDAE4A4ABE1.1205C6E267139B65@lp.airnews.net...

…[9 more quoted lines elided]…

That's interesting, Steve.

I was coming more from the direction of actual on-site development of
in-house applications.

Prior to 1983 when we saw the first PCs changing the world, it would not
have made sense for a mainframe shop to do their in-house development in two
languages. With the advent of client/server networks it has become necessary
to support more than one language anyway, so nowadays there would be less
resistance to the idea.

In the example you cite, where a Bank acquires a package written in another
language, which they may agree to tailor or maintain for themselves, it
makes perfect sense to have a group who can do that, in addition to the
normal application development group.

I believe that today there is much less resistance to the idea of having a
number of language groups on site. The last place I worked in the Midlands
we had VB, PL/1, Web Enablement (HTML/XML) and Oracle development groups all
working alongside each other.

Of course, for smaller Companies, this is much less viable. That's why I
believe component technology will "backfill" the missing areas.

I'm returning to Europe in April and will keep an eye out for sites where
PL/1 and COBOL are both in use. I'd be interested to see what applications
are considered apposite for each language.

Pete.

to be competent in more & more skills, how am I supposed to maintain the
same level of competance? If programmers have always had the spare time to
learn these new skills, why weren't we allowed to be our own DBA's, systems
programmers & business analysts. I suspect that as the skills become more &
more diverse, we will see a retreat in the roles of programmers, to
mainframe, OOP & networking.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol & PL/1

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-11T16:51:19-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9smvge$g14$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3bed066f_7@goliath.newsgroups.com> <20011110091515.26801.00001672@mb-bj.aol.com> <3bedb443_3@goliath.newsgroups.com> <0F8282FFFD147787.0CB23FDAE4A4ABE1.1205C6E267139B65@lp.airnews.net> <3beeea1c_9@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```
My experience in the US (both in the Chicago area and the San Francisco
area - working for banks and utility companies) in the late '70s early '80s
was that almost ALL *LARGE* IBM mainframe shops had BOTH COBOL and PL/I and
BOTH CICS and IMS.  There weren't many (but almost all shops had SOME)
single applications that used both - but within a programming shop (of
multi-hundreds of programmers) working on different applications - these
were done using the "different tools".  My PERCEPTION was that this was not
the "best tool for the job" - but which "buzz-word which manager had heard
when starting a new project".

In fact, one of the MAJOR reasons that IBM came up with LE (originally
"common execution environment) was that it was SUCH a pain to try and figure
out what had happened when a mixed COBOL-PL/I application ABENDed (add IMS,
BTS, and DB2 and EVERYONE fought to get control of "clean-up").

The phone company (AT&T) before divesture REQUIRED that each of the 'local"
companies supported both COBOL and PL/I (with a smattering of FORTRAN).
This was for "centrally developed" applications that local companies
tailored to meet their needs.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ re: COBOL & PL/1

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Scott Peterson <scottp4.removethistoreply@mindspring.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-13T23:12:07-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ef34vt42t9vkv35r971s3npmm64t4ghum7@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<3bed066f_7@goliath.newsgroups.com> <20011110091515.26801.00001672@mb-bj.aol.com> <3bedb443_3@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```
"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> However, it always had to
>compete with COBOL and that is why there was a "division" between shops that
>used COBOL and shops that used PL/1. It really had little to do with the
>relative merits of either language; more to do with IBM pressure and
>marketing.

I think it had everything to do with the fact that mixing PL/1 and
COBOL is a real pain in the neck. I've used and like both languages,
but in the early days, even into the ninties, setting up and
maintaining both a PL/1 and COBOL run-time environment was confusing
and special steps had to be taken to make sure that they didn't step
on each other. Debugging a PL/1 subroutine abend in a COBOL run unit
was difficult because of the way PL/1 used the registers.  Internally
it did not use the standard IBM linkage. Storage management in PL/1
was beyond strange.  Special care had to be taken to make sure the
correct compiler options were used.  There was a whole chapter in the
PL/1 programmers guide on how to pass and receive arguments from
COBOL.  Tables were stored differently.  PL/1 supported data types
that Cobol didn't and didn't do a lot of type checking. Rounding and
truncation and alignment issues between modules written in different
languages were deadly and difficult to debug. 

Other than that it was a lot of fun. <G>  

                         
                         		Scott Peterson
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol & PL/1

- **From:** Volker Bandke <vbandke@bsp-gmbh.com>

On Sat, 10 Nov 2001 23:44:30 +1300, "Peter E. C. Dashwood"
<dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>PL/1 was originally PL/I (Programming Language International) but IBM
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>us are still waiting for PL/2...<G>)

Picking some nits:

The original name of PL/I was NPL, for New Programming Language.  But
these initials were already taken by the British National Physics
Laboratory, and therefore IBM renamed its baby to PL/I
And it is PL/I, not PL/1  (i.e. with a I as a Roman number 1), as seen
on the very first PL/I manual published by IBM (and all the others
published later)


I am not going to comment on the rest, I don't want to get into COBOL
vs PL/I Programming Language Flame War

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol & PL/1

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol & PL/1

- **From:** "Warren Simmons" <warren.simmons@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-12T17:21:22+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<myTH7.183344$W8.6446497@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<1s_G7.115814$B72.30976955@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <3bed066f_7@goliath.newsgroups.com> <jn1vut4gco6dtcist0tdj0bl5pff5orbt5@4ax.com>`

```
Volker,

I don't blame you for wanting to avoid a flame war on this subject.  Yet,
something of interest on the subject
may be of interest.

Before PL/I was introduced, the COBOL project had proceeded to the point of
implementation. Users were getting on
board. IBM had a member on the COBOL Committee.  There was a Guide
organization for the business users of
IBM computers.  Share was only (or primarily) interested in the scientific
applications of IBM computers. Let it be said that every user decided what
group they belonged to, but IBM marketed both "kinds" of computers even
though both could do both kinds of work.  Still there was one line of IBM
computers that was names COMMON.  They did all kinds of
work on smaller systems from IBM.

Share had a committee chaired by a large soap manufacturer that worked with
IBM to define PL/I. There was cross
group communication  in IBM on this effort.  One might say that the effort
was to shoot down COBOL.

These are not mere speculations on my part.  One day I was invited to attend
a meeting of the SHARE committee along with
a few other people from other companies currently supporting COBOL.  The
meeting was scheduled for one day. Object of the meeting was to gain our
support of PL/I in place of COBOL. Upon arrival we were given copies of the
spec, and asked to review them for the meeting on the next day.  We were to
point out road blocks from our perspective.  A few "complaints" made it into
the first PL/I, but no serious review could have been made in the time
allocated. It smelled of political maneuvering, and those of us from the
invited group came to one conclusion, "would you believe PL/2?"  At one time
someone had buttons made to express that opinion.

This was another time when united effort would have paid off for everyone
involved.  Yet, marketing has it's methods.
They are slow to learn, too.

Warren Simmons

Volker Bandke <vbandke@bsp-gmbh.com> wrote in message

crowd.  I feel they had not been

provide the FORTRAN extensions.  Each time I think of that, I grin ear to

In article <jn1vut4gco6dtcist0tdj0bl5pff5orbt5@4ax.com>,
Volker Bandke  <vbandke@bsp-gmbh.com> wrote:
>
>The original name of PL/I was NPL, for New Programming Language.

According to Jean Sammet's book Programming Languages:  History
and Fundamentals, the original name of PL/I was FORTRAN VI.
The name FORTRAN V was used for an internal IBM project.  The
name was changed to NPL around the time blocks were added to the
language.

					Sincerely,
					Bob Corbett

On Sat, 10 Nov 2001 23:44:30 +1300, "Peter E. C. Dashwood"

>It is indeed unusual to find them both on the same site...

I work at a bank and we have some entire systems written in PL/1. But,
at least the area I work in, no new PL/1 code is being written, even
in the formerly all PL/1 systems - Cobol is used instead. I believe
it's due to the fact that we're lucky if we can get entry level
programmers that know Cobol. For PL/1, it's either learn on the fly or
in-house training classes. I have a friend that started at the same
time I did as a newby programmer (5 yrs ago). On her first day, her
team leader handed her a PL/1 book and said learn it - her system was
a pure PL/1 system.

BTW, it is true that older shops have a little bit of everything. We
have Cobol, PL/1, Assembler, Focus, Easytrieve, MRCS (from McDonald
Douglas software!), Culprit (some god-awful report writing package),
Eagle 88 (some not-quite inhouse utility programming language - I find
EZT easier if I want to program something easy without using Cobol).

And of course, things are written in ancient dialects of these
languages. It was only Y2k that got us to get rid of OS Cobol & PL/1
1.4 code. FWIW, our policy seems to be is that we stay current with
Cobol, by being a relase or two behind the current IBM Cobol compiler
for the mainframe.  We did at the beginning of the year stamp out the
VS Cobol II loads we had (i.e recompiled 'em) when IBM ceased support
of the Compiler.

Interesting, Sff5k....

When we learned C-10 on the Univac 1, it took six weeks of full days of
class room work, and six more of learning
how the hardware worked.  Computer operators had to attend another classs of
16 weeks of how to operate.

After that, our learning curve was such that we didn't think we could begin
to program for about a year. Thanks to
on site Steve Wright, and others, we had help.  All of our I/O was given to
us, and modified for our record size, etc.
A couple of years later, a new group arrived, and the consensus among the
first group was that a week of instruction and lots of initial help would do
it.  Memory is not too good on that time period.  I remember being assigned
to teach one person
who had special project knowledge.  That person never learned to code as he
was a project leader.

One class I taught was done having these four take apart the gross to net
program which had been written by Steve Wright.
Up until that time, he had to be flown in to make changes.  Some programmers
never learn to follow procedures, and
perhaps buy an early promotion or transfer.

Warren Simmons
