###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: LOCAL vs.WORKING-STORAGE (was: Finding LOCAL-STORAGE and/or BLK cells)

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-20T04:11:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<AJkK7.118744$WW.7436621@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<_ruJ7.173437$3d2.7303551@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <20011119005530.07128.00000647@mb-bh.aol.com> <3bfa8c0e.344301638@192.168.2.2> <9tc55a$1msmn$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de>`

```
Various respondents replied:
> >  >Wow!. Out of curiosity, why are you static linking this many progams?
To avoid the LINK SVC overhead on a batch job that may run several million
records.

> >  >>we've
> >  >>cut down our overall storage use by 6MB (about 80%) and cut CPU time
by
> >  >>about 20%.  This is because local storage is made available to other
> >  >>programs on return from a call,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> >  >local storage is unavailable,
Local storage is stack storage.  Working storage is heap storage.  Working
storage values are retained for possible future calls.  Local storage is
logically freed on return from a call, and is available to the next program
to be called.
> >
> > Not quite Steve:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > although its not convienent.
No. For local storage, results are unpredictable after a call to another
program.  Suppose program A calls program B and program C.  After the call
to program B, the contents of program B's local storage may still be valid,
though I'd say that was unlikely as the COBOL termination routine (IGZETRM)
also uses stack storage.  After the call to program C, they are certainly
not, as that storage has now been used by program C.
> >
> > Remember, he is doing a static link.  Had he done dynamic
> > link with call/cancel it would be gone, and vastly more
> > saving of space would be obtainable.
No.  The maximum space used is the total used in the calling chain with the
largest stack, rounded up to the next allocation level.
> So what do they get from using local storage on the static call?  What
> accounts for the storage and CPU savings?  I thought the CALL/CANCEL did
> most of that...
Every time you call a re-entrant COBOL subroutine, the COBOL space manager
(IGZESPM) searches the storage chain index (serially) to see if storage has
already been allocated for this program.  If so, it sets the pointer to the
existing storage.  Otherwise it allocates a new area and adds it to the end
of the chain. Stack storage saves the address of the NAB (next available
byte) in each stack frame, so there's no search requirement.

Please, if you want a discussion of static vs. dynamic calls and LOCAL vs
WORKING storage, reply to this thread.  If you know the answer to the
original question, please reply to the original message.
Thanks,
D de J
```

```
> logically freed on return from a call, and is available to the next program

Would one of you mainframe gurus kindly provide a short example of using Local
Storage. Seems like the O/S has beaten the Standard on this one.

I can't comment one way or the other on non-OO but I constantly use Local
Storage in OO methods - and there for SURE - leave a method and the 'local'
values are lost, until such time as the method is re-invoked. If however a
value calculated in the method is stored in Global W/S - then that value
remains as 'active' until such time as the particular class is terminated
(finalized).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Finding LOCAL-STORAGE and/or BLK cells

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-22T07:14:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BFCA5BF.8EAE508E@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<_ruJ7.173437$3d2.7303551@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <20011119005530.07128.00000647@mb-bh.aol.com> <3bfa8c0e.344301638@192.168.2.2> <9tc55a$1msmn$1@ID-39920.news.dfncis.de>`

```
DBuck wrote:
> >
> > Remember, he is doing a static link.  Had he done dynamic
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> most of that...

When a CANCEL is done the whole subroutine's memory [may] become
available for use by other programs, though probably not when statically
linked.

Local-Storage (in my systems) in a sub-program is created anew for each
CALL and is released when that program does an EXIT PROGRAM, even when
static linked.  It may be created on the stack, or in a 'heap'. It is
vital that the only assumption made about local-storage is that it
_will_ be junk values when the sub-program is entered.   

In re-entrant programs (such as under Windows) each new entry creates
another local-storage (which is why it is stack based).
```

## Thread: GUI migration — 6 msgs

_Topics: [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### GUI migration

- **From:** "Wolf Grossi" <X-news@magro-soft.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T09:08:35+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1006153314.167349@hagakure.utanet.at>`

```
Hi folks,

    maintaining a large cobol application with over 600 dialog progs using a
special character based screen-handler.
Then progs are runnung on unix/linux using MF-cobol and 130 nt-workstations,
which will be repaced by linux in the next future.

What are the possibilities to replace this screen-handler with a
gui-interface?

I've heard about MF NetExpress and about flexus.

Thanks for reading and your opinion,
Wolf
```

#### ↳ Re: GUI migration

- **From:** Paul Barnett <paul.barnett@microfocus.nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T10:40:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
Wolf Grossi wrote:

> Hi folks,
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Wolf

NetExpress has a GUI version of Dialog which works in Windows 95, 98, ME 2000
and XP. The version of Micro Focus COBOL on Linux is Object COBOL Developer
Suite and this has only character based tools. You could use it to develop a
client/server application, using Gui Dialog on a Windows PC and the server side
code being on Linux or Unix.

Have you condsidered developing a browser based interface? This can be
developed in NetExpress and deployed on Unix/Linux. No browser plug-ins are
required to use this unlike other products I could mention.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: GUI migration

- **From:** Thane Hubbell <nospam@newsranger.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T22:18:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9zfK7.30793$xS6.51247@www.newsranger.com>`
- **References:** `<1006153314.167349@hagakure.utanet.at> <3BF8E1B1.8197748@microfocus.nospam.com>`

```
Thanks for the reminder Paul - Flexus also has Web Client - use the same (with a
few restrictions based on what the browser can do) sp2 panels and COBOL source
code and have a browser based interface.  Even having developed several
applications this way, I prefer the Thin Client.


In article <3BF8E1B1.8197748@microfocus.nospam.com>, Paul Barnett says...
>
>Wolf Grossi wrote:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

#### ↳ Re: GUI migration

- **From:** Thane Hubbell <nospam@newsranger.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T16:10:04+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<w9aK7.30313$xS6.48390@www.newsranger.com>`
- **References:** `<1006153314.167349@hagakure.utanet.at>`

```
Since you have a custom screen handler, Sp2 from Flexus is probably your best
bet.  Conversions from custom screen handlers - where you own the code and are
familiar with it - seem to be much easier.  Sometimes you can replace portions
of your handler with calls to sp2 and get there.  Without knowing the handler, I
can't say for sure.

Interestingly, with sp2 you can keep your system running on a Linux server, but
give your users a Windows based GUI by using the Flexus Thin client product.
This product has been around for several years and is quite robust.
Additionally, it allows you to use any COBOL compiler you desire.

PS - I do tech support for Flexus on this product.
```

## Thread: Backups are for wimps... — 22 msgs

### Backups are for wimps...

- **From:** dashwood@enternet.co.nz (Peter E. C. Dashwood)
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T02:24:46-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b3638c46.0111190224.62fc465e@posting.google.com>`

```
Three days ago my 3 month old 20GIG Hitachi hard drive crashed.


As a result I am posting this on a 486 Notebook hastily brought out of
retirement and re-configured, but still running under Win 3.1... I am
unable at the moment to get mail from the accounts I normally use in
CLC, so I apologise for the backlog of private mail which is
accumulating; as soon as I can get my main Notebook back on the air
(outlook about a week) I will acknowledge all mail. (My private mail
address is still accessible, for those of you who know it, but I won't
be publishing it here).

The supplier has agreed to replace the disk under warranty, but the
last full backup was taken in Melbourne nearly 3 months ago... (I am
therefore sweating it to try and recover as much as possible from the
crashed disk...an interesting exercise which I would not wish on
anybody. I take back all the swearing I've done at Windows; being
forced to go back to DOS for the recovery exercise has made me realise
that the contest between CLI and GUI is a "no contest". Despite being
facile with DOS after using it for many years, There is no comparison
between a tree view of directory structures with expanded long file
names, and having to painfully traverse and deduce directory
structures from the 8 character DOS names...)

For those of you have not had the extremely unpleasant experience of
losing your primary hard disk, let me tell you, you don't know what
you've missed.

You start with a feeling in the pit of your stomach which is kind of
similar to being hit in the solar plexus with a medium size
sledgehammer...<G> After the intial shock you try to keep calm and
think about what can be done. It's not possible to have rational
thought,as images keep flitting into your mind of the essential data
that you have quite probably lost, and as fast as you suppress them
and try to concentrate on the job in hand, more of them arrive, like
repeated blows with a hammer... The whole experience is reminiscent of
one of those fairground "Whack a Mole" games, where
```

#### ↳ Re: Backups are for wimps...

- **From:** "JerryP" <news@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T13:17:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<mD7K7.45605$2w.2255612@bin4.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<b3638c46.0111190224.62fc465e@posting.google.com>`

```

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:b3638c46.0111190224.62fc465e@posting.google.com...
> Three days ago my 3 month old 20GIG Hitachi hard drive crashed.
>

We, too, recently began having problems with the hard drive in our server.
Fortunately, the NT system detected the early failures and started fussing.
The drive had been in service for almost three years (after it replaced a
failed drive which, too, had been in service for three years).

Even with backups, one is faced with from one to several days to set things
right: get new drive, install OS, install updates to OS, install drivers,
install programs, reconfigure, passwords, etc., THEN restore the backup.

Our server now has TWO drives and a RAID controller running in MIRROR mode.
Every write to the drive is replicated, via the hardware path, to the second
drive; reads are split between the two drives (providing faster access and
transfer rates). But here's the neat part:

When one drive fails, or starts getting flakey, the controller detects the
condition and starts running on the spare. Meanwhile, the controller sets up
a din to warn everybody of impending doom unless the suspect drive is
replaced. We hot-swap the bad disk using drive caddies (about $12). The
controller applaudes our move and begins rebuilding the bad drive to match
the existing drive as a background task. This rebuilding, depending on
system load, may take as long as four hours. Meanwhile, during all this
to-ing and fro-ing, the system stays online.

This is, of course, not a substitute for a rigouous backup scheme; but
MIRRORING does prevent the crisis when a drive fails.

You need: 2 identical hard drives (20G = $80/each), a Promise Technologies
RAID controller (about $80), and, optionally, one or two drive caddies ($12
each). With THREE drives, you can routinely swap the secondary drive and let
the controller re-mirror the spare. This gives you an almost instant backup
of the whole operating system.
```

```
servers.  It's a real comfort to know that the probablility of loosing my C:
drive is almost nill.

Of course it's still not available on Laptops, so I'm afraid Pete would
still be dead in the water.  You have my sympathy Pete - Been there, done
that, burned the damn T-shirt.
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
>
> The bad part is that people STILL don't back up their data on PCs and
> workstations.  Hardware failure is not the only reason that we lose data.
I
> bet it isn't even the primary reason.

1. We had a customer who was at the epicenter of the San Francisco
earthquake (Hollister, California). His three-story building collapsed on
the computer. Then the building (rubble, actually) caught fire. Then, I'm
not making this up, about a million gallons of tomato paste from a warehouse
across the street oozed over and extinguished the fire. But his disaster
wasn't over: he then called us.

2. Another customer in South Carolina had his computer stolen three times in
ten days. Every time he got a new one, the burglars went back to the well.

Moral: in addition to failures of software, hardware, and humans, one still
should account for acts of God and acts of the Godless.

Backups are good; getting the backups out of the building is better. Best is
sending the backup, via the internet, to one of these file storage
companies.
```

```
That's one of the reasons that I mirror my stuff on 2 computers,
2 disks each, and a recursive data-comparison over everything
every other day.

I have computed that the probability of loosing everything by
the simultaneous crash of all four disks is far less than the
probability of an asteroidal impact. In fact, the probability is
dwarfed by the probability of myself getting mad and execute
a "rm -r -f /" with root privileges - this is likely to happen as
frequently as every other thousand years.

Herwig
```

```
do it using GUI classes and dynamically creating the table of
entryfields. I broke my own rules - as it was a first attempt.  I didn't

! It worked, but would have been a sod to maintain.

Surprise, surprise ! When I extracted from the zipfile, Winzip had cut
off the source two thirds of the way through the program. I forget the
actual message but something like "Source exceeds ??K". It wasn't a
major disaster as much of the changing I was going to make occurred it
that lost "third".

Curious if anybody else has hit a similar problem with zipping.
```

```
I normally back up monthly to a tape streamer but, as I thought I was

didn't think it would be necessary to lug a tape streamer and a bunch of
disks in addition to my trusty Notebook. That 4 months has turned into 2
years and it is really just as well that everything was backed up in
Melbourne before installing the new 20 Gig drive.

I'll be investing in an external hard drive and mirroring the internal one.

substitute for an image copy. (Rebuilding is never the same and it is a
tedious and heartbreaking exercise). The other thing that this exercise
reminded me about is the value of friendship... I would never have recovered
without the freely given help of several people, and one in particular.

hope it made you think about Backups...
```

## Thread: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release — 12 msgs

### Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "Gunnar Opheim" <gunnar.opheim@eunet.no>
- **Date:** 2001-11-19T23:39:46+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`

```
I have a problem understanding a warning expressed in a Share session, see
last page of
http://www-1.ibm.com/servers/eserver/zseries/zos/le/conference/pdf/smn8231.p
df

In short it says that heap storage should be allocated above the 16Mb line
for a program compiled with RENT, DATA(31) and run with HEAP(ANYWHERE), but
that it was allocated below the line before LE R2.9.  This could cause
problems when programs linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24) calls another 24-bit
program.

Question: Why should it ever be allocated above the line if the program is
linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24)?

regards,
Gunnar.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-21T02:22:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pdEK7.119549$WW.7540014@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`

```
This is one of those situations where you may have done something in
violation of the rules, but it happened to work because of the way the
system was implemented.
If you have a (AMODE 31) program that acquires above the line storage, and
you pass control to one that is executing in AMODE 24, it will crash when it
tries to access a passed parameter that is physically above the line.
Previously, there was a good chance that the "anywhere" storage was
physically below the line, even though it had a 31-bit address (with the
high-order bits zeroes), so the 24-bit program could access it with no
problem.  This will not be the case in the future.
D de J

"Gunnar Opheim" <gunnar.opheim@eunet.no> wrote in message
```

```
> D de J

I am aware of that.  But this is not what the warning says.

"After LE 2.9 HEAP for programs compiled with DATA(31) and runtime option
HEAP(ANYWHERE) will be allocated above the line. This can cause problems
with programs that are linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24) and in turn call another
24 bit program. The resolution is to make sure all 24 bit COBOL programs
that are calling 24 bit programs are compiled as RENT and DATA(24)."

So I repeat my question: Why should it ever be allocated above the line if
the program is
linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24)?

I am concerned because I have suggested that if the program (compiled with
DATA(31) is linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24) we will always be safe calling a
24-bit program. Only if linked AMODE(31) RMODE(ANY) and calling dynamically
a 24-bit program will it fail.  With static call, and letting the binder
determine AMODE/RMODE, we will get the appropriate mode depending on the
subroutine's mode.

regards,
Gunnar.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-21T14:16:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<GGOK7.199361$W8.7476474@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <pdEK7.119549$WW.7540014@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <9tfiq8$1qq$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`

```
The data allocation depends on the compile-time options.  The addressing and
residence mode depend on the link-time options.  You can compile a program
any way you like, but if you specify MODE AMODE(24) or MODE AMODE(31) at
link time, that is how program fetch will pass control.
D de J
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Gunnar Opheim" <gunnar.opheim@eunet.no>
- **Date:** 2001-11-21T21:15:57+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9th1p1$feb$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <pdEK7.119549$WW.7540014@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <9tfiq8$1qq$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <GGOK7.199361$W8.7476474@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
If the load module is linked with AMODE(24) and RMODE(24) do you really
believe that data will be allocated above the line when the program is
compiled RENT and DATA(31)?

I have tested this.  The cobol main program is compiled on Cobol for 390
compiler with RENT and DATA(31).  I calls an assembler subroutine (static
call).  We let the The Binder determine the resulting mode (not specified).
Runtime option HEAP(ANYWHERE).  The subroutine reports the address of the
parameter list and the address of the parameter.

When the assembler subroutine is AMODE(24) RMODE(24) the resulting load
module becomes AMODE(24) RMODE(24), and the parameter list is below the line
and the parameter is also below the line (it points to the Cobol program's
Working.Storage).

When the assembler subroutine is AMODE(ANY) RMODE(ANY) the resulting load
module becomes AMODE(31) RMODE(ANY), and the parameter list is below the
line and the parameter is above the line (it points to the Cobol program's
Working.Storage).

This has been tested with Language environment from R2.8 and R2.10 with
identical results.

So the test contradicts the 'warning'.  It also contradicts your statement
that the data allocation only depends on the compile-time options.  However,
the data goes where I would expect it to go.  The only thing I do not
understand is why the parameter list is below the line in the last case.

My concern is about the warning.  Have I misunderstood what it says, or is
it simply wrong?

Gunnar.
```

- **From:** david <dskirk@usa.net>

```
this is a complicated issue and was a major cause of confusion even in mid 
'80's during migration to COBOL II. i've written on this before, but there 
are often nuances that are unique. i think the main items are a matter of 
misinterpretation. when books and error messages refer to compiling with 
RENT and DATA(31), there is an assumption that the AMODE and RMODE will not 
be changed at link time. with that assumption, the mentioned error message 
is correct.  

also, i agree that AMODE and RMODE are really set at link time, blending 
the modes of the linked modules. normally, i've found that most assembler 
modules that are 24/24 can be reassembled as 31-bit with little effort.

to summarize, i think the message is correct if the stated compile options 
carry through to the executable. 
david
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-23T02:58:05+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1XiL7.180322$3d2.7945678@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <pdEK7.119549$WW.7540014@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <9tfiq8$1qq$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <GGOK7.199361$W8.7476474@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <9th1p1$feb$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`

```
It's not a question of what I believe, it's what the programming guide says:

"For reentrant programs, the DATA(24|31) compiler option and the HEAP
run-time option control whether storage for dynamic data areas (such as
WORKING-STORAGE section and FD record areas) is obtained from below the
16-megabyte line or from unrestricted storage.  (The DATA option does not
affect the location of LOCAL-STORAGE section variables; the STACK run-time
option controls that location instead.)"

There is no mention of whether this is affected by AMODE.

(I agree it doesn't make sense, but that's true of a lot of things about
LE).

The parameter list is below the line because it is allocated from stack
storage, and you probably aren't overriding the STACK(BELOW) default option.

I think there is one thing you are misinterpreting:  AMODE doesn't change on
a static call.  A dynamic call will switch to the mode of the called
program.

Actually, the more I think about this the less sense it makes.  Even if the
24-bit caller did have storage allocated above the line, how could it
address it?

It would make more sense if the caller was in AMODE(31) and dynamically
called an AMODE(24) program.  Under the old implementation it would be OK as
long as the passed storage was actually located below the line.  The problem
would come in the new implementation, when the called program tried to
access storage which is now physically above the line.

I'm copying John Scull to see if he has any comments.

D de J
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-27T12:51:01-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9u0nc3$rhc$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no>`

```
Lots of people have replied - and the answers have missed what the REAL
problem is (and when you will see the symptoms).

The problem occurs when you have a "new" COBOL program with RENT,DATA(31)
(compiler-options - and default linkage editor options) that does a DYNAMIC
call to a program that is linkedited as AMODE(24) - and the "above the line"
program passes data to the "below the line" program.

Up thru LE R2.9 in some (many? most?) cases, the storage for the DATA(31)
program was ACTUALLY obtained below-the line.  (This meet the
documentation - which only said that storage MIGHT be obtained above the
line - not that it always would be).  Therefore, when the "above the line
program" passed data (CALL ... BY REFERENCE) to the "below the line"
program, everything "worked fine" - even though you were running in an
"unpredictable" situation.

With LE R2.9 and later "preference" to DATA(31) storage was to ACTUALLY
obtain it above the line.  This meant that when you had the EXACT same
dynamic call situation, the "below the line - amode(24) - program" would NOW
be receiving data that really did require 31-bit addresses.  This would
result in "same application - no recompile - getting S0C4 - or worse" - just
because LE release was changed.

The solution to the problem is:

A) recompile the "above the line" program with the DATA(24) option to force
ALL of its storage below the line (so it can be passed to the amode(24)
program).
   or
B) "fix" the below the line program, so it can be CORRECTLY link-edited
AMODE(31).
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T01:06:29+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pMWM7.239008$W8.8231731@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <9u0nc3$rhc$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
No.  You have missed the point of Gunnar's original question, which was:
> > Question: Why should it ever be allocated above the line if the program
is
> > linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24)?
This was a quote from the original SHARE article.

On 11/22, I replied:
> It would make more sense if the caller was in AMODE(31) and dynamically
> called an AMODE(24) program.  Under the old implementation it would be OK
as
> long as the passed storage was actually located below the line.  The
problem
> would come in the new implementation, when the called program tried to
> access storage which is now physically above the line.

Which is exactly what you are saying, except you have overlooked what the
original article said, and apparently didn't bother to read my posting of
11/22.

D de J

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message

>
> The problem occurs when you have a "new" COBOL program with RENT,DATA(31)
> (compiler-options - and default linkage editor options) that does a
DYNAMIC
> call to a program that is linkedited as AMODE(24) - and the "above the
line"
> program passes data to the "below the line" program.
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> dynamic call situation, the "below the line - amode(24) - program" would
NOW
> be receiving data that really did require 31-bit addresses.  This would
> result in "same application - no recompile - getting S0C4 - or worse" -
just
> because LE release was changed.
>
> The solution to the problem is:
>
> A) recompile the "above the line" program with the DATA(24) option to
force
> ALL of its storage below the line (so it can be passed to the amode(24)
> program).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for OS/390 Heap storage and LE release

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-27T20:40:30-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9u1is1$hja$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9tc1g8$977$1@oslo-nntp.eunet.no> <9u0nc3$rhc$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net> <pMWM7.239008$W8.8231731@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
The original note says,

"In short it says that heap storage should be allocated above the 16Mb line
for a program compiled with RENT, DATA(31) and run with HEAP(ANYWHERE), but
that it was allocated below the line before LE R2.9.  This could cause
problems when programs linked AMODE(24) RMODE(24) calls another 24-bit
program."

As this was a SUMMARY  ("in short") of what the SHARE presentation said, I
have replied assuming that it is the last sentence that is error (either in
the summary or the original presentation).  The PROBLEM (which is well
documented by IBM) occurs when a RENT,DATA(31) program (dynamically) calls
an AMODE(24) program.

This worked before LE 2.9 (in most cases) and fails with 2.9 and later (in
many/most cases).

If this still isn't clear, please provide the exact quote from the SHARE
presentation *and* the author - and I will research it further.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Diff between IMS GN and GNP ?

- **From:** topmind@technologist.com (Topmind)
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T07:42:08-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4e705869.0111260742.7c2b9a5e@posting.google.com>`

```
Dennis Galion <WBXMRPYGVKBM@spammotel.com> wrote in message news:<hccqvt4brr36jl1fa4ahskp5ivdafkqlpb@4ax.com>...
> Here's a fairly good description.  
> 
> http://cobolreport.com/cobolreport/archives/imscode.htm
> 

I believe I have seen that example. Although a good
general example, it does not really hilight
the *differences* between GN and GNP that I can
tell.

From what I gather so far, GNP does *not* set
"parentage", but is bounded by it. 
IOW, there *is* a secondary "pointer",
called "parentage" (at least informally). GN and
GU can set (move) the parentage pointer, but GNP
can *not*. It can only react to it. The parentage
pointer acts as a "ceiling" where GNP cannot
go above or around. GNP is limited to the
branches below the parentage pointer and
can not get "out" of that branch unless
the parentage is changed by a command that
can change parentage.

If this depiction is not accurate, please
let me know.

but, is it limited to one level below the
parent, or *any* level below the
parent (on the same branch)?

Thanks, and thanks for the other book suggestions
above also.  

Interesting. Who ever thought the dot-com
bust would take me into the mainframe world.

-T-

> 
> Dennis
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> >>
```

## Thread: Writing ASCII files to upload to an EBCDIC mainframe... — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Writing ASCII files to upload to an EBCDIC mainframe...

- **From:** samiel@samiel.com (Samiel)
- **Date:** 2001-11-20T15:23:55-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b763d5b3.0111201523.38036a12@posting.google.com>`

```
Hi.  I've searched the groups and I couldn't find an answer, so sorry if this
has already been asked.

I need to write an ASCII file for upload to an EBCDIC mainframe.  Generally
the file is converted seemlessly from ASCII to EBCDIC when the lines I write
are of the form (COBOL):

X(6)
9(6)

...but I have come across a COBOL PIC (picture) I am not sure how to write
in ASCII, namely:

S9(3)

How would write the following numbers in ASCII following the above COBOL
PIC?

0
1
-1
10
-10
100 (not possible?)
-100 (not possible?)

My specifications state the ASCII should take up 2 characters (bytes).

Any help would be appreciated.  Thanks!

- Samiel

p.s. ASCII HEX is preferable for me to figure this out!
```

#### ↳ Re: Writing ASCII files to upload to an EBCDIC mainframe...

- **From:** Ed Guy <ed_guy@paralynx.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-20T15:56:54-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BFAEDC6.2A48@paralynx.com>`
- **References:** `<b763d5b3.0111201523.38036a12@posting.google.com>`

```
Samiel wrote:
> 
> Hi.  I've searched the groups and I couldn't find an answer, so sorry if this
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> My specifications state the ASCII should take up 2 characters (bytes).

Then they are expecting to find the data as "packed" decimal (the COBOL 
spec probably says USAGE=COMP3 as well as giving the picture).  This means that two 
digits are squeezed into each byte (except the last, which has one digit and the sign 
bit). This results in characters which get converted as if they were the equivalent 
one-byte text character when you IND$FILE or KERMIT them to the EBCDIC machine. When 
they get broken apart in COBOL on the EBCDIC machine get don't get the numbers you 
started with.

My ParseRat product handles this going from EBCDIC to ASCII but you might have to write 
something to do it going the other way.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Writing ASCII files to upload to an EBCDIC mainframe...

- **From:** "Ron" <NoSoy@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-20T22:34:05-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9tfas0$5m9$1@suaar1ab.prod.compuserve.com>`
- **References:** `<b763d5b3.0111201523.38036a12@posting.google.com> <3BFAEDC6.2A48@paralynx.com>`

```
If you're using MicroFocus compile the PC program with the
($set sign "ebcdic") compiler directive. When the file is
uploaded to OS/390 and converted from ascii to ebcdic the
sign overpunches will then convert properly in stardard
zoned decimal format.
```

#### ↳ Re: Writing ASCII files to upload to an EBCDIC mainframe...

- **From:** Steve Thompson <s_thompson@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-21T18:57:15-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BFC3F5B.CE57A1DE@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<b763d5b3.0111201523.38036a12@posting.google.com>`

```
Samiel wrote:
> 
> Hi.  I've searched the groups and I couldn't find an answer, so sorry if this
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> PIC?

Just out of curiosity, the source system wouldn't happen to
be a WANG VS would it? If it is, do yourself a favor and
just put the file out to the mainframe in binary. On the
mainframe do the following:

//STEP1    EXEC PGM=IEBGENER
//SYSPRINT DD SYSOUT=*
//SYSUT1   DD DISP=SHR,DSN= <<Fully qualified name of
uploaded Data Set
//SYSUT2   DD DISP=(,CATLG,DELETE),DSN=&&TAPE,VOL=SER=
<whatever your shop dictates
//SYSIN    DD DUMMY
//*
//STEP2    EXEC PGM=?????  <= Your program for processing
//ddname1  DD DISP=(OLD,PASS),DSN=&&TAPE,DCB=OPTCD=Q
//ddname2  DD DISP=SHR,DSN= <<Same as SYSUT1 from STEP1
        .
        .

Now, if you set your COBOL program to use the same copy
books as were used on the WANG VS, then here is what happens
when you read from ddname1 & ddname2 (keep both reads in
sync): the area holding the record read from ddname1 will be
translated from ASCII to EBCDIC. The record read from ddame2
will have all the packed decimal data read for you to put
where ever you'd like.

I used to do this kind of thing when converting from a WANG
VS to IBM systems back in the '80s.

Otherwise, why don't you try changing the picture to "SIGN
SEPARATE FOLLOWING" (I think that's the correct clause) on
the ASCII machine?
```

```
runcob recover2 <return>

INDEX FILE : <name file to convert>
DROP FILE : a name of ypur choice (ex. PIPPO.seq)
OPTIONI : NOSUB
```

```
DISPLAY "HELLO, WORLD".

I try to analyse an old COBOL-program, because i have to read
COBOL-generated data with an other program.
The problem : I don't know COBOL very well, and the programmer who wrote the
tool is not employed here any more. no documentation is available, of
course.

source-code.

The problem a can not solve at the moment is that:

Definition from the input-file in the code:

FD  FILEIN           BLOCK CONTAINS 0 RECORDS
                     LABEL RECORDS ARE OMITTED
                     RECORDING MODE IS V.
01  FILEIN-RECORD.
    05 NO1    PIC    S9(13) COMP-3.
    05 TXT    PIC     X(03).
    05 NO2    PIC    S9(07) COMP-3.
    ...


The beginning of the input-file (first 14 bytes in hex)
01 00 10 00 EC 10 A9 45 55 58 00 11 01 5E
+------------------+ +------+ +---------+

The TXT is "EUX", i can read, and that is correct as far as i know.
BUT HOW DO I READ THE f*** NUMBERS??
It should be backed decimals (COMP-3), but they are not, as far as i can
see..!

Thanks for any tips,
Matthias
```

#### ↳ Re: Problem with Data-Structures/Formats

- **From:** "JerryP" <news@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-23T01:50:01+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cXhL7.17285$YD.1610538@news2.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<9tj1hp$2to97$1@ID-109843.news.dfncis.de>`

```
>
> Definition from the input-file in the code:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> see..!

45 55 58 = EUX
00 11 01 5E = +11,015

It's the "01 00 10 00 EC 10" that's goofy. I think you're screwed.

There IS another clue: "RECORDING MODE IS V(arible)." Most (many) compilers
put control information at the start of each block/record when
variable-length records are involved. These extra bytes tell the program the
size of the following block/record and the initial seemingly-bizarre string
may very well be this control information. If so, then the 1st eight bytes
may be the block/record control information and your 13-digit NO1 variable
is 45,558,001,101+. A few more bytes of the record should confirm or
confound this hypothesis.

By the way, it's "Packed," not "Backed."
```

#### ↳ Re: Problem with Data-Structures/Formats

- **From:** "giovanni" <gioxb@libero.it>
- **Date:** 2001-11-24T00:00:14+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9tk043$35a$1@nreada.inwind.it>`
- **References:** `<9tj1hp$2to97$1@ID-109843.news.dfncis.de>`

```
Hi matthias, comp-3 usually depends un compiler used, in ANSI-85 it means
"packed-decimal storage" and "two digits are stored in each byte. An
additional half byte is allocated for the sign, even if the value is
unsigned.  The sign is placed in the rightmost position, and its value is
0x0D for negative; all other values are treated as positive (...).  The size
of an item (including one for the implied sign) is divided by two to arrive
at its actual size (rounding fractions up)" (from Acucobol manual). One way
is to find a utility (usually included with cobol runtimes) capable to
convert file to ascii text.
bye gio

"Matthias Philipp" <wrongmail@web.de> ha scritto nel messaggio
news:9tj1hp$2to97$1@ID-109843.news.dfncis.de...
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Problem with Data-Structures/Formats

- **From:** Charles <nospam@newsranger.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-27T16:31:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<RdPM7.39531$xS6.65010@www.newsranger.com>`
- **References:** `<9tj1hp$2to97$1@ID-109843.news.dfncis.de> <APnM7.37702$xS6.60684@www.newsranger.com>`

```
>>Definition from the input-file in the code:
>>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
>visit us at: http://www.winterthur.com
*****IMPORTANT:******
Recording mode V is for variable length records.  These records have control
fields that can precede the record or segment of a record.  They require special
processing.  It is also possible that something like the file format could have
been changed since the program was written.

The best way to transfer data from the mainframe to the PC is to copy the
contents of the file to a temporary file in the file transfer area that has no
packed data and then ftp the file to the PC.  There are some mainframe products
like file-aid and DITTO that could print out or let you view the data to see
what is there.  A report writter could print out a few lines to let you see what
the data looks like before you download it.

COBOL isnt that complicated.  To unpack a field you just have to have a variable
like "05  NEW-NBR-1     PIC 9(13)" and then you move the packed data to the new
decimal data field.  It will then take up more space.  Of course, if the data is
bad (not numeric), when you move it the program would crash, unless you tested
it for numeric.  That is just a little harder to do.

It is possible that when the author of the program wrote it, there was more than
one program run to achieve the process.  Sometimes we use 2 jobs to achieve
something and run the first job and then the second job.  It is also possible
that the first record is a Header Record, that is not meant to be used.

Might be nice to know what type of file it came from, how you are downloading
it, what compiler you are using, what type of mainframe, what Operating Sytem on
the mainframe(Fujitsu, Honeywell, IBM, OS2, S390, VM, VSE/ESA, etc.).  If you
had the source code for the program you might be able to hire a consultant to
help you.
```

<a id="t-2f78925649"></a>
## Thread: XMLBooster for Cobol - Press Release — 1 msgs

### XMLBooster for Cobol - Press Release

- **From:** Deborah Torrekens <deborah@raincode.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-23T13:13:03+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BFE3D4F.F26319EC@raincode.com>`

```
http://www.xmlbooster.com

XMLBooster for COBOL - Press Release - November 2001
* * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * * *

XMLBooster is an XML parser generator which is used
to integrate XML parsing services in COBOL applications.
Version 2.3.11 supports:

- Generation of high-performance parsers (XML to a COBOL
  data structure) and unparsers (COBOL data structure
  back to an XML character stream) in the form of plain
  COBOL copy books to be included royalty-free in
  application programs.
- XMLBooster-generated parsers take the incoming XML
  messages (or files), and fill an easy to use, strongly
  typed COBOL structure that can be used by application
  programmers without XML-specific knowledge.
- Generation of standard COBOL code, that can be used
  with most compilers on most platforms, including Win32,
  Unix, MVS and VMS.
- Support for user-defined entities.
- Support for regular expressions which are used to
  validate incoming data at parse time.
- Generates HTML-based cross-referenced documentation,
  explaining the intricacies of the XML format it parses,
  far more efficiently than plain DTD's.
- Improved support for schemas and DTD's.
- Supports COBOL, C, C++, Java and Delphi for convenient
  multi-language infrastructures.

More information available at http://www.xmlbooster.com
```

## Thread: SPF Pro and Ventura Publisher on Windows 2000 — 6 msgs

### SPF Pro and Ventura Publisher on Windows 2000

- **From:** scomstock@aol.com (S Comstock)
- **Date:** 2001-11-23T15:06:15+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20011123100615.09782.00001240@mb-fz.aol.com>`

```
I have recently "upgraded" one of my PC systems from Windows 98 to Windows 2000
Professional.

Most of my old applications still work just fine, but there are two that no
longer work and I was wondering if anyone has experience / suggestions that
might help here.

First SPF Pro from CTC. This is an ISPF look-alike for the PC. It's been
discussed on this newsgroup various times in the past. 

Over the years I have purchased / upgraded to several versions: 

       SPF PC which is DOS based; it still runs under Windows 2000, but cannot
handle long names

       SPF Win which is Windows 3.x based; it runs, but for some reason will
not always
                open directories that have members

       SPF Pro which says "for Win-95 and Win-NT" on its installation diskette;
when I try to get
                this running, I get the message 

"The application failed to initialize properly (0xc00000005). Click OK to
terminate the application."

My problem is that this is no longer the current version of the product. CTC
have moved on to later versions, but these later versions do not support REXX,
only C. So I would like to keep SPF Pro, but no support and no ideas (I've
uninstalled and reinstalled several times). Any help would be appreciated.

The other app I would like to keep is Ventura Publisher 4.1. This is also way
down level (current version is 8.x, I think). But when VP was purchased by
Corel they changed all the interfaces, forced you to change file naming
conventions, and provided no reasonable migration path (I have hundreds of VP
chapters written for my course materials). The strange thing is, VP worked
under Windows 2000 at one point, then as I was updating drivers and adding
Visual Age COBOL and PL/I, VP stopped working; I have tried uninstalling the VA
products, but still no go; so it must be a driver, and I can't figure out how
to find which driver is causing the problem. 

Of course, no one from the relevant companies cares (not Microsoft [although I
have found tech support from them to be vastly improved], Corel, nor CTC).
Since these are no longer supported products, there's no incentive for them to
bother, and I understand that.

Any suggestions appreciated, including suggestions for related newsgroups to
try.

Kind regards,


Steve Comstock
```

## Thread: COBOL FAQ — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Meta: FAQs, group policy, charter`](topics.md#meta)_

### COBOL FAQ

- **From:** wmklein@ix.netcom.com (William M. Klein)
- **Date:** 2001-11-24T11:28:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol,news.answers,comp.answers,alt.answers
- **Message-ID:** `<computer-lang/cobol-faq_1006601284@rtfm.mit.edu>`

```
Archive-name: computer-lang/cobol-faq
Posting-Frequency: twice a month
Last-Modified: 2000/07/02
Version: 3.01
URL: http://www.cobolreport.com/faqs/cobolfaq.htm
Reply-To: wmklein@ix.netcom.com


                            COBOL FAQ
                  (Frequently Asked Questions)

Last-Modified: Sunday, July 2, 2000

The COBOL FAQ has moved.  To view the most recent version, please check out the
web version at:

  http://www.cobolreport.com/faqs/cobolfaq.htm

If you have comments, corrects, deletions, or additions, please send private
email to:

    wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com

The following is Appendix B.5 - Changes to create Version 3.01

 o Recorded new location of the FAQ
 o Updated some old (bad) web links
 o Added some new COBOL books (particularly those written or contributed to - by
frequent comp.lang.cobol submitters).
 o Added information about LegacyJ (PERCobol) and changed the company name from
"Synkronix" to "LegacyJ".
 o Updated information for changes in product and company names and addresses
where available
 o Updated the URLs for downloading the draft of the next COBOL Standard and
CobCy
 o Started the Samples/Example Section
 o Tried to get rid of most specific references to Windows/NT, Windows/95,
Windows/98, and Windows/2000.
 o Added links to Xinotech
 o Added information on Norcom and GUI ScreenIO
 o Added (back) information on Wang
 o Added (back) information on the Tiny-COBOL ('74 Standard) project
```

```
Your best bet, (if available in Germany), get a copy of Thane Hubbell's
'Teach Yourself COBOL in 24 Hours" - it comes with a free CD containing
Fujitsu COBOL. Not the latest version, but very adequate to get you
started.

As an alternative, should you qualify as a student, you could get Net
Express (Micro Focus) University Edition for about $US100 - check out
microfocus.com.

Go to cobolreport.com and check out Bill Klein's FAQ (Frequently Asked
Questions) - which answers a lot of other questions about COBOL. If you
want a good set of examples, check out the University of Limerick, Eire
-

http://www.csis.ul.ie/COBOL/examples/

There are other sources for examples it you do Web searches on "COBOL".
You will find Thane's examples and those from the U of Limerick cover
most of the groundwork for you.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

## Thread: Cobol and screens — 9 this month / 16 total

### Cobol and screens

- **From:** "Jan Swaelens" <wamd1873@wanadoo.be>
- **Date:** 2001-11-24T17:42:28+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9toine$lbj$1@scavenger.euro.net>`

```
Hi there,

Where can I find some info(syntax) or manuals about programming windows,
buttons, lists and file-output on the screen? This is for a school project.
I am using a Microsoft Cobol Compiler.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol and screens

- **From:** "JerryP" <news@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-25T00:33:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<B%WL7.85796$qx2.5283856@bin5.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<9toine$lbj$1@scavenger.euro.net>`

```

"Jan Swaelens" <wamd1873@wanadoo.be>

> Where can I find some info(syntax) or manuals about programming windows,
> buttons, lists and file-output on the screen? This is for a school
project.
> I am using a Microsoft Cobol Compiler.

There are several ways:
1. Get a book on Windows APIs and figure out how to invoke them from COBOL
(all 3000 of them).
2. Use VB for the screens and link your COBOL and VB together.
3. Get a COBOL compiler that is Windows aware and can communicate directly
with the operating system (Fujitsu V4+ for example).
4. Get an add-on product such as SP2 to do the windowing work for you.

My advice, forget about making your program stylistically swell. There is no
end of people who can make an application look pretty and too few that can
make the application actually work.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol and screens

```
I agree with this wholeheartedly.

sure that you use a 16 bit version of the COBOL GUI development tool.
```

```
You may say that these guys are doing a sales pitch, but I can personally
say that using a GUI screen handler is the best way to create true windows
screens without the learning curve for using windows API calls. Why spend so
much time to learn the windows API when you can be creating very nice
screens with all the bells and whistles in a short time. I happen to use
Norcom's GUI Screenio and in a very short time I was generating sharp
windows programs using COBOL. Download an evaluation and try it for
yourself. By not spending so much time learning windows API calls I have
much more time to sell my services. Bottom line it is better to let someone
else do the hard part and utilize your vaulable time sonewhere else.

Steve
```

```
development...(At least for the type of development that I do)...Mostly
because the developer did not have a way to access the standard windows
message loop using ScreenIO...There are many standard windows development
functions that require access to the message loop...Timers,
sending/receiving messages to/from other programs, Async I-O to com ports,
sockets, pipes, etc...etc...It is not possible to use these functions in
ScreenIO programs, because ScreenIO hides the message loop from the
developer...

Here is a direct quote from one of the folks at ScreenIO -----   "We believe
that GUI ScreenIO does an excellent job of providing functionality while
presenting a simple and easy-to-use programming interface.  If you need to
inspect the Windows message queue and respond to low-level Windows events,
use pipes or deal with tcp sockets at a low level, GUI ScreenIO is probably
not for you."   ...Personally, to date I have not developed a true windows
application that has not had a need to access the windows or OS/2 message
loop/queue...Windows applications have to typically do more than present
screens to the user...If someone needs to develop an application that simply
displays screens to the user, then go for it...ScreenIO might very well do
the job for that person...

Another problem with the product was that it did not support auto-scaling of
windows and controls...If a window is sized by the user, the associated
controls and fonts used within the window should also be scaled and changed
to match the new window size. Hopefully this feature has been added to their
product...

I personally did not like the approach taken for say listbox
controls/combobox controls...Having to pre-define the number of possible
entries...Nor did I like being forced to use a working storage table to hold
the entries in memory...This is redundant and consumes valuable machine
resources...i.e. memory...Windows itself has to keep memory allocated for
entries in a listbox control...Using ScreenIO, the memory requirements for a
listbox are now double what would be required using the standard APIs...I
believe also, that there are maximum size limits for the data in a listbox
or combobox with ScreenIO...When I looked at the product the limit was a max
of 64K worth of data in a listbox...I remember also being presented with 3
or 4 option dialogs to define a listbox that had all options choices
disabled...Why present a developer with a screen to select an option when
all options are disabled? Again, this may have been fixed, but I found it a
waste of my time as a developer to deal with dialogs that should have never
be displayed...

I agree that there is a long learning curve for API development, but once
you have learned to create a window or a button or a listbox, the next time
is simple cut-and-paste...Calling an API to do something is no different
than calling some cobol routine to do something...I have been using API's
for development for many years, and still have not learned them all...Using
native API's to develop true windows applications is very do-able though,
and should not be over looked as a choice for development...

API calls can be encapsulated into OO objects...OR...They can be contained
within subroutines or DLLs...Personally I use a "Common Routines" module for
most API functions...For example to enable a window I use

Call WinAPI crEnableWindow
   using by value hWndWindow.

or

Invoke crEnableWindow
   WindowHandle is hWndWindow.

The code for entry point 'crEnableWindow' is a call or perhaps many calls to
the operating system API(s). I have a common routines module for Windows and
one for OS/2...The actual user app code does not change...I also reduce the
complication of dealing with the API(s) by having routines in the common
routines module that performs one, several or even many actual API
calls...For example opening and initializing a COM port...This might require
5 different API calls...But from my app, I simplify that to a single call :

Call WinAPI crComOpen
   using by reference z'COM1'
         by reference z'19200,N,8,1'
returning wsPortHandle.

Many call(s) get made to the actual operating system APIs, but my app is
coded using a very simple call to the crComOpen entry point. Once these
common routines are developed (and it will take some time to develop) the UI
or interface to OS functions like reading and writing to com ports, etc is
greatly simplified.

Window procedures and callbacks are nothing more than entry points that
Windows calls with associated parameters passed...These can be encapsulated
into separate modules as well...Recursion and messaging is a fact of life in
a windows environment...An understanding of how these things work is a
requirement that cannot be avoided no matter how you handle the user
interface...

Someone who is inquiring about what direction to take in their future
development should explore ALL the various options that are available to
them before they make a decision...API programming is one of those options
available and it works very well...Developers should not be intimidated by
vendors of UI products telling them how complicated API programming is, when
in reality, it is not really that complicated to provide a GUI interface,
ONCE the learning curve is completed and messaging, recursion and multiple
entry points is understood...Programming in windows or OS/2 is going to
require a learning curve regardless of the UI method employed...Be it API
calls directly, OO invokes and property settings, Activex, or ScreenIO or
SP2...There is still a learning curve to be dealt with...The original poster
will need to decide which method he/she wants to use...His decision needs to
be based on fact finding and asking questions and getting responses...

What is a programmer going to do when he encounters something that cannot be
done by a 3rd party UI product? He is going to need to understand how
windows or OS/2 works and that there are APIs to do what the 3rd party
product(s) cannot do...Does ScreenIO deal with reading and writing com
ports, semaphores, mutexes, pipes, TCP sockets, file sharing, networking,
shared memory between modules, threads, displaying graphics, automatic
scaling of windows and dialogs for different screen resolutions or maximized
vs a normal window, owner drawn controls, compression and expansion of files
or data, etc? These are common windows or OS/2 functions that will
eventually be needed in a lot of business applications...May as well get
started with the learning curve now...

BTW, I am not opposed to using 3rd party products by any means...I use the
NE dialog editor, ListPro, InputPro, TabPro, SocketTools, the Victor Graphic
libraries, etc on a daily basis to make my life easier...The nice thing is
all these 3rd party products work just like calling the normal windows
operating system APIs and they conform to the normal windows programming
methods (message loops, recursion, sync or async processing)...I am not
restricted because my windows program is based entirely on a specific 3rd
party UI product...There is NO single 3rd party product that can accomplish
what all of the various tools and operating system API's can accomplish...

I will be happy to send anyone interested, a copy of my evaluation of
ScreenIO which includes their responses to the problems I found with the
product...The product has most likely changed since my evaluation, but my
initial impressions and experiences (including several abends) were bad
enough to keep me from looking at it again...

Personally, I do not think that product vendors should be posting comments
like

<<< That's a fairly significant undertaking do it the level you seem to be
suggesting. There is a huge investment in infrastructure just to get to the
place you can put a button out.">>>

This is nothing more than an attempt to make programming via API's sound too
difficult to do and to sell their product...You can "put a button out" in a
single simple call to the windows API...Folks from this same company have in
the past also made commits such as "you would have to be crazy to use native
windows API's"...If they want to sell and support their product here,
great...I'm all for that...I'm sure there are potential customers here...But
they should not be making absurd commits in an attempt to intimidate someone
away from alternative ways of development, simply to push their own 3rd
party product...

Of course, this is simply my opinion...You and others are certainly free to
voice your opinions as well...Maybe together, we can answer the original
posters question...

kenmullins
newnan, ga USA
```

---

#### ↳ Re: Query on HP 3000 COBOL

- **From:** leclaire <leclaire@cinci-rr.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T21:52:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C02B871.5FF1B599@cinci-rr.com>`

```
No experience with the 9000, but I did work in a couple shops on the
3000's.  Their COBOL had a few quirks relating to the machine's word
size (I recall lots of agony over an even number of bytes).  

There was no implementation of the screen section and instead you made
calls to HP's "View/3000" subsystem, which itself had some nice
features, but still it was outside of COBOL and probably not easily
transported to other platforms.  Screens were built and invoked via
CALLS.  The folks from HP who formed Tandem carried this same madness
with them in the form of an entire other language called SCOBOL or
screen cobol.  

Also, I recall HP's ISAM utility also had a few quirks.  I think they
called it RSAM, or was it KSAM?  But that's not really unusual, as every
implementation has its oddities.  I was however impressed with the
processing power of that system.

As far as conversion to IBM, I can't help you there.
```

#### ↳ Re: Query on HP 3000 COBOL

- **From:** Beat Gossweiler <beat.gossweiler@dp-solutions.ch>

```
Steve,

I know both OS/390 COBOL and have written some code on a HP3000. The HP
COBOL is pretty much straight forward. One of the major differences is
that most of the system-functions are implemented as so called
'intrinsics', which are calls to a system API. Also, of course, their
Database-System needs some special handling.

When i did the work mentioned their compiler the company used was not
yet ANS85, which meant no END-IF etc.

Regards,
Beat Gossweiler
DP Solutions AG
Geroldswil, Switzerland
```

```
Instead of telling us what type of techniques that you are familiar with in
another language, which probably uses different terminology than a COBOL
programmer would not be familiar with, why not just say what you want the
program to do in American English.

For instance what do you mean by "K-MAP or tabulation method"?  

Not all COBOL Compilers have a Boolean type variable.

If you have 20 variables you want to check it may be preferrable to divide the
tasks up into smaller sub-tasks and handle them separately.  In COBOL we would
sub-divide the tasks into paragraphs or routines.  These routines are sometimes
called procedures.  (The COBOL compiler turns them into callable modules).  Also
in Standard COBOL, the variables are Global.

If you prefer object oriented languages there are some COBOL compilers that are
more object oriented.

Just tell us what you are trying to do.  We can't read your mind!  Drop the
terminology you are comfortable with and just spell it out in simple language.

Charles
```

#### ↳ Re: how to implement boolean function

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T12:05:53+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c041e4c_6@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<d336983f.0111260335.6ef45f72@posting.google.com>`

```
(Please note the spelling of "Boolean"; it is named in honour of George
Boole, a 19th Century English mathematician who postulated an algebra based
on two values, long before computers as we know them were ever thought of,
and who happens to be a hero of mine...<G>)

I am assuming that you want to have say, 20, variables which can all
represent two states (could be binary bits, but not necessarily). You wish
to return a TRUE or FALSE indication if ALL of these variables are in the
TRUE state.
If this assumption is incorrect then you really will need to post your
requirement in simple, clear, English (American or otherwise...<G>)

Binyamin asked why you would want to do this and I endorse his sentiment...
I guess the answer is "Because it is an exercise and I need to do it". I can
see little practical application.

Thane mentioned the use of EVALUATE...ALSO. This is a good solution but
becomes unwieldy as the number of variables increases.

The following solution allows up to 128 variables. (It can easily be
extended if you need more...)

To accommodate COBOL the variables are passed as a string of bytes. Each
byte can be x'F1' representing TRUE, or x'F0' representing FALSE. Unused
bytes will be x'00'.  (You can arbitrarily set these values to anything you
like as long as you maintain consistency).It would also be possible to pass
a list of individual variables, but this would be tedious and, as each
variable can only represent 1 of two values, it's a bit unnecessary. The
solution could be easily modified to accommodate a string of binary values
if the platform you are running on has bit operations available to COBOL.
What is here will run on any platform supporting COBOL 85.

WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.

01   finished-flag  pic x value space.
       88 finished  value '1'.
       88 not-finished value '0'.


LINKAGE SECTION.
01   in-variables pic x occurs 128
                                  indexed by in-x1.
01   return-value  pic x.
       88 return-TRUE   value '1'.
       88 return-FALSE value '0'.


PROCEDURE DIVISION using in-variables, return-value.
...
       set not-finished to TRUE
       set return-TRUE to TRUE
       perform varying in-x1
                                from 1
                                   by  1
                                 until  in-x1 > 128 or finished
            evaluate in-variable (in-x1)
                 when x'F0'
                      set return-FALSE to TRUE
                      set finished to TRUE
                 when x'00'
                      set finished to TRUE
            end-evaluate
       end-perform
       .
return-to-caller.
       goback.

There are a number of enhancements that could be made to the above (it could
be modified to return the position of every variable which was not TRUE, for
example) but I think you will catch the drift.

The literal 128 should really be a variable and some versions of COBOL allow
it to be set at compile time so that any number of input 'variables' can be
accommodated easily with a single recompile and minimum amendment to the
code. The stuff above could be edited to replace '128' with anything you
like.

Similarly, the input string could be replaced with a binary string in COBOL
that supports it. It would then not be possible to recognise 'unused'
variables... COBOL cannot pass dynamic variables (the length of the passed
string cannot be varied wthout re-compiling).

Hope this helps and good luck with your studies.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: how to implement boolean function

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T17:01:52+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c046731_2@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<d336983f.0111260335.6ef45f72@posting.google.com> <3c041e4c_6@Usenet.com> <MZXM7.40224$xS6.67091@www.newsranger.com>`

```
John,

Neither of us is drunk... (however, I'm working on it..<G>)

This is a contrived example where I was trying to address a number of
things...

I DID actually consider something very similar to what you've suggested. I
have answered your question as why it wouldn't work below... But it isn't
really important. I believe the approach to the solution is more important
here.

 The fact is that there could be any number of variables being input. There
is also a possibility of a stochastic input string (like, the first, third,
and sixth, seventh, and eighth, bytes are variables but the others are
ignored. It has "gaps" in it, if you like...).

My code, as posted, doesn't handle this either.  However, the code I have
posted could be easily amended to handle it... here's what it would take...:

> >       set not-finished to TRUE
>>        set return-TRUE to TRUE
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> >                      set finished to TRUE
[removed 2 lines here]
> >            end-evaluate
> >       end-perform

The code you have provided would need more significant amendment.

What if, instead of a string of bytes, the input variables were an array of
numbers, each number representing a TRUE or FALSE or unused (Don't care...)
state?

What if the solution has to be enhanced to provide a list of the actual
parameters which were FALSE (or TRUE, for that matter...)? I think I
mentioned this in the original post.

The idea of looking at each individual input variable is to support any of
these requirements. ONLY when we have something which indicates by its state
that it is FALSE (as opposed to NOT TRUE) can we actually give up and exit.

I was in two minds whether to post this because I'm not convinced the actual
requirement has been stated (and consequently, understood) properly.

some comments below...

John H Sleight Jr <nospam@newsranger.com> wrote in message
news:MZXM7.40224$xS6.67091@www.newsranger.com...
> Hi Peter,
>
> I must have read this 5 times and came to the conclusion one of us must be
drunk
> <G>.
> Changing one element of your scenerio. Unused bytes will be x'F1'.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Well, if the in-variables were [= all '1'], it would mean there were no
variables present...You said you wanted x'F1' to represent "unused" <G>. In
this case you would  correctly return FALSE. But in every other case you
would return TRUE. So even if just one variable was present and its state
was FALSE, you would still return TRUE....

> I know, it can't recognize an unused variable set, but you don't report
that
> condition anyway.
>
The code above DOES report an unused variable set [all '1']. My code would
require an additional test for this. As it stands, my code will incorrectly
report TRUE if no variables are passed.

The danger with using "all '1'" Jack, is that it presumes the collating
sequence on the target platform will recognise x'F1' as a numeric 1.  We
could just as easily use x'FF' and test for high-values... The only way to
be certain is to recognise the three possible states (TRUE, FALSE, NOT
INTERESTED) with a specific hex value. That way we don't run foul of
different architectures and collating sequences.

Now, I know that "extreme programming"calls for ONLY solving the problem at
hand and taking no thought for the morrow, but, being of the old school,
experience has taught me that exploring a problem before coding a solution
enables a code structure which can save future pain, for minimal additional
effort. It's a bit like defensive driving; you start off doing it
consciously, then after a while you do it automatically. So, at least as far
as this goes I can honestly claim not to be "extreme"...<G>

Now, where did I put that Jack Daniels...

Cheers!

Pete.

> Regards, Jack.
>
```

## Thread: Tab-delimited output file — 2 msgs

### Re: Tab-delimited output file

- **From:** leclaire <leclaire@cinci-rr.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T21:41:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C02B5CA.770F12C5@cinci-rr.com>`
- **References:** `<91af75c4.0111260713.39764d2b@posting.google.com>`

```
Stefan Meyer wrote:
> 
> I just tried to create a tab-delimited output file (which should be
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Microfocus refers to this as "null insertion" and it is one of the
annoying "features" of MF Cobol.  I think there is a (-N) runtime switch
to suppress this behavior.  As I recall, this is well documented if you
dig thru the manuals, but it's not obvious at first.  You may also be
able to suppress it by coding some "call x"nn" in your program, but it
is strictly a runtime function and cannot be permently turned off by any
compiler directive that I know of.
```

### How to sequentially update? ANY help is great!

- **From:** "Kevin" <kevin23100@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T23:49:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ByAM7.51296$gQ1.20692659@news1.elmhst1.il.home.com>`

```
I have been doing some basic COBOL for a little while now but most of it has
been basic fixing of errors for those new to it...nothing to advanced.

I am familiar with the what goes into a sequential update type of program
(master file, transactional file, and the updated master file that results
from the two, but how would this exactly be intergrated into a data and
procedural division? What would be the most general or basic form. I have
been trying to use the following basic data to update in a sequential
manner....just so that I can get a grasp of this new concept to me..

Master File containing an ID and a Balance
A transactioanl File containg an ID that correlates to one of the ID's under
the master file, a single letter code (pic x) that determines to either add,
subtract or delete the specified ID and then another transactional field
that contains an amount to either ad or subtract.

Thank you anyone for ANY help that you can offer me!

Kevin
```

#### ↳ Re: How to sequentially update? ANY help is great!

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-11-26T19:44:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ceBM7.6338$cC5.928026@news20.bellglobal.com>`
- **References:** `<ByAM7.51296$gQ1.20692659@news1.elmhst1.il.home.com>`

```
you are making a *very* basic error that is common to programmers being
introduced to accounting for the first time ... probably because an
accountant tried to program. Never, never, never subtract in accounting, and
never, ever introduce a variable that acts as a pretend sign.  You will
regret it forever.

You have Debits (negative entities) and Credits (positive entities).  Those
are added together to create another debit, credit, or balanced item.  That
is it.  There are no other types of entry.  All else is documentary.
```
