On 29-Nov-2001, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> It works just fine, but after a year's use we realize the above
> computation
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> method in a large application.

Let's say your computation needs to return a different type of result.   Say
your subcodes now are alphanumeric, your zip codes now are 10 digits, or can
now contain spaces, you now pass a country code, or the table you return is
now larger.  You can come up with many similar cases.

Typically with called programs, some will work, some will break.  Sometimes
the change can be subtle, as someone made an unwarranted assumption in a
called program that worked until now.   Maybe there was an implicit
limitation that was used (a bug became a feature).  Are objects immune to
this type of breakage?  Or will they need to be tested.

Sure not all changes need such extensive testing.  But programmers tend to
error on the wrong side here.

Thanks for the detailed description of that program.  I have printed it off
and will peruse it as I get time.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IBM announces new release of z/OS (OS/390) compiler

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Warren Simmons" <warren.simmons@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T02:10:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xUBN7.241681$W8.8474471@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<9u0lg9$jcb$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <9u0mer$1vl$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <3c04255b_5@Usenet.com> <281120012151462287%josephk@iinet.net.au> <3C05A457.1142E5DE@shaw.ca> <0YrN7.86817$8q.11538966@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <3C06A7D2.6D4C7627@shaw.ca>`

```
James,

Your detail though still a little fuzzy in  my old tired mind calls to
attention something we did on the Univac I.

Grace would say, oh, we did that, we just didn't have a name for it.

Here is my example for your review.

The world of DP is mainly TAB, with it's restrictions.

The printer is 150 lines a minute, and has no programmable control.

Forms are blank pages, and reports are listings with some blank space in
either direction.

We now have a U I.  It has all kinds of features we never dreamed of...

The boss says, why can't you make that report look like it came from a
typewriter?

Oh, oh, we can.

Using extraction, shifting, etc. we can make any field look like it was
typed.

Well, we can but it takes more memory than we have.

That's O.K. We will write a routine that does it once for any numeric field
up to a certain size.
To use it, move the number to the "input field" of the routine.
Execute the routine.
Grab the results from the allocated place, and move it to where you need it.

=====

Like I/O in those days, routines like that were in all programs that needed
them. You didn't code them
if someone else had already done it. You copied it manually.  The biggest
problem with this kind of thing.

Now, substitute a uniform copy, and all do this procedure the same way.

With Flowmatic, I/O became the first standard routines in my memory.

Question?

Am I getting the idea?

Warren

the

> > called program gets changed.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IBM announces new release of z/OS (OS/390) compiler

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T05:43:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C071D08.F8564B24@shaw.ca>`
- **References:** `<9u0lg9$jcb$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <9u0mer$1vl$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <3c04255b_5@Usenet.com> <281120012151462287%josephk@iinet.net.au> <3C05A457.1142E5DE@shaw.ca> <0YrN7.86817$8q.11538966@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <3C06A7D2.6D4C7627@shaw.ca> <xUBN7.241681$W8.8474471@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```


Warren Simmons wrote:

> James,
>
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes Warren you are getting real close.

However those sub-routines - you copied them manually. Think on the word "class"
= classification, where you group like-minded routines into one class. A good
example would be metric/imperial conversions. One of the problems with OO is the
academics talk in terms of classes as being a hierarchy, an animal, four legged,
a cow, elk, moose, God knows what else. Like so many people I react "What the
hell has that got to do with dollars and cents or inventory levels ?"

>That's O.K. We will write a routine that does it once for any numeric field
>up to a certain size.
>To use it, move the number to the "input field" of the routine.
>Execute the routine.
>Grab the results from the allocated place, and move it to where you need it.

Now that to my mind is real close to Pete's components concept. I'm not gong to
steal his thunder - let him expand if sees fit.

Let me back-track on a couple of things from other messages :

1. Thane's suggestion of a sub-class, and I came up with :-

>            ISAMFile inherits from Base
>            ISAMSubClass inherits from ISAMFile

>  invoke os-ISAMSubClass "readPrimeKey" using xx returning xxx.

Thinking on that last line - that's terrible, every program accessing would have
to be changed from "invoke ISAMFile" to "invoke ISAMSubClass". Nothing really to
do with the OO methodology but to illustrate its flexibility.

a) Leave ISAMFile as is - don't change anything
b) introduce a new ISAMSubClassFile with only the pertinent methods required to
extend the operation.Calling programs requiring this have the syntax :-

    invoke ISAMSubClassFile "get-new-method" using .... returning .....

c) Don't like (a) and (b) - 1. Rename ISAMSubClass to ISAMFile, and 2. Rename
ISAMFile to ISAMSuperFile :-

        ISAMSuperFile inherits from Base (the original ISAMFile)
        ISAMFile inherits from ISAMSuperFile (this is a renamed ISAMSubClass
                with a couple of new methods)

So now we compile these two files. Your calling programs still contain :-

    invoke ISAMFile "this-method" using......returning.......

First port of call for your caller is ISAMFile - your method name is one of the
'old ones' so the message bypasses ISAMFile and finds what it wants in
ISAMSuperFile. If it is one of the new messages then it is going to be found in
the current ISAMFile.

I'm not suggesting you should do it this way - but it indicates the flexibility
- one recompile on the renamed file and a new compile for the latter.

2.  File handling - two approaches, mine culled from Will Price (objectz.com)
and Don's - both are valid given the options in OO COBOL. Let's take Don's from
what he has written :-

[Program 1]
[Program 2]    ..................each of these invokes....[ ProductFile]
[Program 3] etc....

As Don indicated, ProductFile has methods such as following :-

method-id. open
method-id. read
method-id. write
method-id rewrite
method-id close etc....
then :-
method-id. increment invoice addition
method-id decrement invoice usage
method-id find all 'particular' records (just like an SQL cursor and SELECT

DISTINCT)
method-id. move from 'a' to'b'
method-id etc....

A self-contained handler for the Product file accessed by 300 programs if
necessary. He needs a new method - so, (1) add the method to Product file and
(2) invoke it from a new program. That source change only affects the two
programs noted.

Now my version, slightly different twist. Recall me saying the FileClass doesn't
do any decision making - just contains methods for file VERBS. Back to Don's
diagram above; what I didn't include for Howard, to avoid confusion, was my
DBI(Data Base Interface) a 'go-between' the calling programs and ProductFile :-

[Program 1]
[Program 2]    ......each of these invokes..[ ProductDBI]...which in turn
invokes
[Program 3]
etc....
{ProductFile}

My Product file is the 'bare bones' containing just the file VERBS. Don's
"extras",
Methods - increment invoice addition,  decrement invoice usage,  find all
'particular' records, move from 'a' to'b' are contained in my ProductDBI,
together with methods to create collections/dictionaries, (primarily for
droplists/listboxes). Note Don invokes ProductFile direct - I don't, I invoke
ProductDBI - and if it doesn't find the method, the message further searches in
super (ProductFile).

Program 26 contains :-

    invoke ProductDBI "createCollection" using..... returning........

        within the ProductDBI :-
        method-id. "createCollection"
    --------------------------
        set FileNotFinis to true
        perform until FileFinis
            invoke super (ProductFile) "readNext" returning ls-results
            if not FileError do something with the returned record
       End-perform
        when finished return the collection to Program 26.

Having taken that approach, then I realized that my file handling, (I originally
had a separate class for ProductFile, OrderFile, CustomerFile etc.) could be
vanilla flavoured and 'generic'. That's where Bill K. helped when he suggested
:-

    01 Myrecord.
         05 pic x occurs 0 to xxxx depending on ws-CharacterCount

So I was able to take it one step further - get rid of my individual File
classes and substitute ISAMFile class. I still have the DBI classes because they
contain methods specific to each  particular file. Example, CustomerDBI is
invoked for "getAddressData" - this gets the complete record from CustomerFile
but strips off just the address info which is returned to the calling program -
and to get this right the Caller and CustomerDBI contain a copyfile for address
data.

Just illustrates, like all things COBOL, how different approaches can be
effective.

3. Howard made some queries :-

> Let's say your computation needs to return a different type of result.   Say
> your subcodes now are alphanumeric, your zip codes now are 10 digits, or can
> now contain spaces, you now pass a country code, or the table you return is
> now larger.  You can come up with many similar cases.

I would need to know specifics to answer some of the above. But some 'off the
cuff'.
This one jumps immediately to mind 'your subcodes now are alphanumeric'. I have
exactly that situation. The old RM/COBOL system contained two digit numeric
codes to tell me points being inspected, e.g. :-
01 = Top
02 =  Bottom
03 =  North
04 = South
44 = Scan Low Reading
48 = Weld Seam Pitting
49 = External Pitting

Fine, if you can remember numeric codes - but for newcomers ? After some ten
years of a static system the user then threw a zinger - 44 and 49 above were
'exception conditions', along with some others. Lordy, lordy - build a table to
check for 'exceptions', (echoes of Eileen's lousy account numbers <G>)..

So in the revamp I decided to use mnemonics :-
T    = Top
B    = Bottom
XSL = Scan Low Reading
WSP = Weld Seam Pitting
XEP = External Pitting

And to cover those exceptions I started them with an "X". Converting from RM to
MF I search a table and translate from numeric to uppercase Alpha. Same goes for
Howard. If you have  :-

*>-------------------------
method-id. "gertPointName"
*>-------------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-alpha                    pic x(04).
Linkage section.
01 lnk-key                    pic x(04).
01 lnk-name                 pic x(20).
Procedure Division using lnk-key returning lnk-name

if lnk-key(1:1) is numeric
   invoke self "convertCode" using lnk-key" returning ls-Alpha
    ("convertCode" is a PRIVATE method in the same class as this method solely
        there to serve this particular method)

 else move lnk-key to ls-Alpha
end-if

 invoke os-DescripsDBI "getName" using ls-Alpha returning lnk-Name
*>-------------

Other points Howard made were changing the size of descriptions. Well that
really isn't manageable by anything is it. That goes back to the original
design. The file record contained 60 characters and now the record contains 83
characters. You are talking a different file structure, so firstly a file
conversion followed by a check on all programs accessing the file.(Again that
N/E Search for inclusion of a method-name helps here).

'The table you return is larger.....". That's not a problem if you are using
collections/dictionaries - as I illustrated elsewhere - they are open ended :-

    - create MyCollection
    - invoke MyCollection "size" returning ls-size.

Jimmy
```

### Re: Need help with a Cobol Lab

- **From:** JAndersen@screenio.com (John Andersen)
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T01:18:26+00:00

```
The general response form comp.lang.cobol group will be to do your own
homework.  By that we don't mean we to say we will not help, but 
we are generally not willing to take the raw assignment and do it
for you.  You have to at least make an effort, and when you get
stuck then ask a specific question.

After all, one of us might hire you when you graduate, and
we would like to know that you know what you are doing.

So take a stab at it and come back when you get stuck.

> find "interesting") is the following,

<a id="t-1c789b84df"></a>
## Thread: implementation boolean function — 2 msgs

### implementation boolean function

- **From:** monaa_v@indiatimes.com (mohan)
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T03:43:52-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d336983f.0111280343.5f10bb7c@posting.google.com>`

```
This issue related to "SET THERY". How can I simplify complex 
expression. 
For example, We can simplify the expression ((A ^ B') ^ C) U (A ^ B) in 
to A ^
(B U C).
That is,
	((A ^ B') ^ C) U (A ^ B)
next	(A ^ B' ^ C) U (A ^ B)
next	A ^ (( B' ^ C) U  B)	
next	A ^ (( B' U B) ^ (C U B))	
next	A ^  (C U B)
Finally	A ^  (B U C)
 


Here we have only there variables .It is trivial if I  have more than 20 variables. 


Could any one know the general algorithm?

                                       
                          Thanking  you                regards
                                                       Mohan
```

#### ↳ Re: implementation boolean function

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-11-29T11:59:30+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3c0571fd_5@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<d336983f.0111280343.5f10bb7c@posting.google.com>`

```
Mohan,

thanks for the clarification.

Your set theory examples are a "longhand" form of Boolean Algebra.

It "translates" as follows:

U = union = OR = +
^ =  intersection = AND = .
'  =  negation = NOT = '

What I believe you are looking for is an algorithm for Boolean
Simplification of a complex expression.

As far as I know, there is NO general algorithm to do this. However there
ARE some basic rules of Boole's Algebra and these can be stated as follows:

1. It is commutative for addition and, consequently, multiplication. (note
that AND represents multiplication and OR represents addition..)

A.B = B.A    A+B = B+A

2. It is associative for these operations also.

A.(B.C) = (A.B).C   A+(B+C) = (A+B)+C

3. It has a multiplicative identity. (1 and 0 represent TRUE and FALSE)

A.1 = A    (literally, "A AND TRUE = A")

4. It has an additive identity.

A+0 = A  (literally, "A OR FALSE = A")

5. It has a NEGATION operation.

A'  (this leads to an interesting effect... A.A'=0  A+A'=1... A AND NOT A is
ALWAYS FALSE, A OR NOT A is ALWAYS TRUE)

6. There are 2 further Laws which were formulated by the French
Mathematician, De Morgan, and are therefore known as "De Morgan's Laws"...

De Morgan 1:

(A.B)' = A' + B'    (A AND B all negated, is equivalent to NOT A OR NOT B)

Many computer programmers recognise this as "if you negate an AND it becomes
an OR" and vice versa.

De Morgan 2:

(A+B)' = A'.B'     (A OR B  all negated, is equivalent to NOT A AND NOT B)

Given the above simple, basic, rules it is possible to simplify some very
convoluted and complex logic.
(There are some additional rules regarding implication, but these are more
advanced and push the Algebra into something called Propositional Calculus.
Not necessary for what we are considering here...)

Some time ago in this forum "Yukon Mama" (Eileen) posted some COBOL code
that was pretty hideous and taken from a program she was maintaining which
had obviously been over maintained and grown like Topsy.  She asked for
opinions about re-structuring the code and  many of us responded with
various solutions.

Normally, I would have a copy of my post available but it is still in the
process of being recovered so you will need to search the Google archive for
this Group.

It took about 20 minutes to apply the above rules and come up with something
mathematically equivalent (same truth value) but less convoluted.

Now let's have a look at what you are REALLY looking for...

Please ignore the code I posted in response to your request; it doesn't
address this at all and simply shows that I misunderstood your question.

I'll start with your own example (translated to Boolean notation as
described above...sorry, I just feel more comfortable with this form):

 (AB').C)+ (AB) [note that A AND B can be written as "A.B" or simply "AB"]

1. Regrouping by the associative law...

A.B'.C + A.B = A.((B+B').C + B)

2. From Rule 5 above we see that B+B' is always TRUE, so we can write...

A. ((1.C) + B) or simply... A.(C + B)

which translates into "English" as "A AND (B OR C)"

As you noted, this is fairly trivial, but the difference is that each step
of the above is based on the Rules described.

It is the RULES which allow the Simplification, NOT a general Algorithm.

Having said that, you COULD describe a set of operations, based on the
rules, which could be applied to the simplification of any complex Boolean
statement, and with ANY number of variables...(the actual numberof variables
is irrelevant; it is the procedure you follow which will derive the
simplified result).

This would be a bit like what you would do in normal Algebra when required
to solve a complex equation...start by collecting like terms, multiply
through by various factors, etc (forgive me, Maths is not my strong point...
but you understand what I am saying...) It is the PROCEDURE, based on the
rules, which obtains the result.

You have already learned about the use of Truth Tables to check Boolean
simplifications but you are quite correct that these can become unwieldy
when many variables are involved. That's why the use of the rules above,
under program control, can provide a general solution for any number of
variables.

Incidentally, around the time of Eileen's problem I considered writing such
a "Logic Simplifier" to be used as a tool for re-factoring Legacy Code. It's
been done... apparently there are several such products already in the
marketplace.

How would you do this in COBOL?

The same way you would in any other language. Decide the steps for your
simplification procedure and program them.

ANY language that supports AND, OR, and NOT (Boolean) functions could be
used to do these simplifications. (As these operations are fundamental to
the working of a modern computer, it is extremely unlikely that any TRUE
computer programming language would not include them. (I don't include stuff
like HTML as a "computer programming" language, although there are those who
would argue this...<G>).

Once, many years ago, I wrote a computer program which ONLY used the Boolean
Assembler operations. It was for a bet, and the people I wagered with could
not believe that the machine was reading punch cards and printing data
without any standard I/O instructions, ONLY AND, OR, and NOT  and Load and
Store registers. It took me two months in my spare time and the finished
program ran in 4K...(Ah! happy times...but I digress...<G>))

Summarising:

1. I know of no "general algorithm" for simplifying Boolean expressions. (If
you find one, please let me know... I'd be interested to check it out)

2. A general procedure for the simplification of such expressions could be
formulated, based on the rules of the Algebra. This could be the "general
algorithm" you are looking for but I would argue that it isn't. My argument
goes that different people will formulate this procedure differently. (In
the same way that no two programmers will write the same code to solve a
given problem).

3. Your best bet is to study and understand the rules, and annotate your
simplifications showing which rule was used at each stage. Doing this will
help you to see if any patterns emerge in your own approach to the problem
solution.

Hope this helps...

Pete.

mohan <monaa_v@indiatimes.com> wrote in message
news:d336983f.0111280343.5f10bb7c@posting.google.com...
> This issue related to "SET THERY". How can I simplify complex
> expression.
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> Here we have only there variables .It is trivial if I  have more than 20
variables.
>
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>                                                        Mohan

## Thread: Unsigned packed numeric opinion — 11 msgs

### Unsigned packed numeric opinion

- **From:** "Gonzo" <ckhamel1961@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T17:49:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`

```
I'm in the middle of writing a conversion program on a IBM mainframe using
COBOL OS/390, and am needing to deal with a large number of unsigned packed
numeric fields.

This is how I'm handling them.... as an example:

Source field defined as:
15  LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER PIC X(8).

Working storage fields I'm using to access this field:
08  WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER     PIC 9(16) COMP-3.
08  FILLER REDEFINES WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.
  12  WSR-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER  PIC X(8).
  12  FILLER                         PIC X(1).

Procedure division statements so I can access this field:
MOVE ZERO TO WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.
MOVE LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER TO
                           WSR-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.
DIVIDE 10 INTO WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.

I've been dealing with unsigned packs like this for years, and hey, it
works, but might there be a "better way" of doing this?

I've just migrated from COBOL II to OS/390 lately, so perhaps there's a
feature in COBOL that I've overlooked just for these?

Thanks in advance...

Gonzo
```

#### ↳ Re: Unsigned packed numeric opinion

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T19:32:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dZaN7.239714$W8.8322150@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`

```
Without running a test compile, my guess would be that this would generate a
move with offset, rather than a decimal divide.  Try compiling with the LIST
and OPT options.  If you see an MVO instruction, you're OK.  If you see a DP
instruction, you're burning a few extra cycles and you should append a zero
to your original number, move it to a packed field then lose the last byte.
Thus:  X'F1F2F3F4F5F6F7F8' -> X'F1F2F3F4F5F6F7F8F0' -> X'123456780C' ->
X'12345678'
D de J
"Gonzo" <ckhamel1961@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com...

> COBOL OS/390, and am needing to deal with a large number of unsigned
packed
> numeric fields.

I have searched the Cobol manuals for this too, and have not been able to
come up with anything better.  The most effective way of doing it is using
the MVO (move with offset) machine instruction, but I don't think Cobol has
any way of utilizing it.  Your divide statement will crash if the data is
invalid, but you did know that.

Gunnar.

support.  For vendors (and there are a NUMBER) that support this, it is

this - you might want to reference such if/when creating a SHARE requirement
of a "service REQUEST".

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Unsigned packed numeric opinion

- **From:** "David de Jongh" <ddejongh@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-29T03:25:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<AUhN7.134699$WW.8486385@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com> <a_dN7.668$hs2.369985658@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`

```
You can still use the same technique, with a minor modification.  Append a
packed zero to the field, move to a character field, then move n-1 bytes to
a comp-3 field:
X'0000000012345678' -> X'00000000123456780C' ->
X'F0F0F0F0F0F0F0F0F1F2F3F4F5F6F7F8F0' ->X'00000000012345678C'
Did you try running a compile with LIST option?  Again, if it's using MVO,
it's probably faster than the UNPK and PACK that this technique would use.
Divide instructions are usually implemented by iterative subtracts and
shifts, which tends to make them inefficient.
BTW, you can change your "12 FILLER PIC X(1)" to "12 APPEND-ZERO PIC S9(1)
COMP-3" which will save a little on the move of zero.
D de J

- **From:** John H Sleight Jr <nospam@newsranger.com>

```
Hi Gonzo,

If you don't mind losing the hi-ord nybble in the source field you could work
from there. E.g., redefine it to pic 9(016) comp, redefine it again to 
pic 9(015) comp-3. Then:

compute src-fld-comp = (src-fld-comp * 16) + 15

If I didn't mix my modulos, you should wind up with 000000000001234F in 
src-fld-comp-3.

If you can't lose the hi-ord nybble, you'll have to move the source fld to a
larger work fld, but the same principle applies.

Regards, Jack.





In article <Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>, Gonzo says...
>
>I'm in the middle of writing a conversion program on a IBM mainframe using
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…

#### ↳ Re: Unsigned packed numeric opinion

- **From:** "WOB" <wobconsult@sprynet.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-29T17:57:56-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9u6e4j$vof$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`

```
Gonzo,

        If you move your 8-byte BCD value to the first eight-bytes of a
redefined PIC 9(16)V9 COMP-3 field (ensure a low-order X'0F'), move the PIC
9(16)V9 COMP-3 field to a PIC 9(16) DISPLAY NUMERIC field. An MVO is
performed in the interim ASSEMBLER code, followed by an UNPACK into the
DISPLAY NUMERIC field. No decimal instructions that can get you into
trouble.

        I've been doing it this way for a long time now, in MVS and VSE
environments, without any problems.

        Even better yet, try PIC 9(16)V9 COMP-3 to PIC 9(16)V9 DISPLAY.
Byte-09 of the COMP-3 field doesn't even require initialization, as the
compiler automatically inserts a X'0F'.

HTH....

Bill

"Gonzo" <ckhamel1961@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com...

#### ↳ Re: Unsigned packed numeric opinion

- **From:** "Gonzo" <ckhamel1961@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T00:02:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<N0AN7.964$Tq2.500824172@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`
- **References:** `<Os9N7.629$8U4.347424832@newssvr12.news.prodigy.com>`

```

> Source field defined as:
> 15  LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER PIC X(8).
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Okay, folks... this is how I've decided to handle this:

Working storage is now:
08  WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER     PIC 9(16) COMP-3.
08  WSQ-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.
  12  WSR-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER  PIC X(8).
  12  FILLER                         PIC X(1) VALUE X'0F'.
08  WSS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER REDEFINES
    WSQ-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER    PIC 9(15)V9 COMP-3.

Procedure division is:
MOVE LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER TO
                           WSR-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.
MOVE WSS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER TO
                           WS-LARAMST-00-ACCOUNT-NUMBER.

The original code generated the following: MVC, ZAP, DP, MVC, an OI.

The new code generates: MVC, MVI, MVO.

I figure a good improvement has been made here. (I tried the OPT, but it
took over 30 minutes to compile, so I went back to NOOPT).

Thanks for the feedback folks....

Gonzo

PS: Ya know, I just had a wild thought.... how about using STRING? That
might move it down to a single statement. Of course, it'll probably generate
a bazillion assembler statements....
--------------------------------------------------
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus has announced its intention to support COBOL 2002

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T15:29:41-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9u3l2i$bkd$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9u3a8e$id0$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <3C055047.39FAEB74@shaw.ca>`

```
Being "involved" with the draft standard does NOT represent a commitment to
implement it.

IBM has *explicitly* avoided any statement on when or IF they will implement
it.

Neither Unisys nor Compaq (aka Tandem, Dec, and future HP) have indicated
publicly a plan to implement it.

Fujitsu HAS talked about all the developers that they have, but they have
NOT stated when/if they will implement the new Standard (in either their
Japanese or US products).

Similarly, NOT being on the committees doesn't mean that vendors won't
implement it.  AcuCorp, CA, PerCOBOL, etc have been equally "quiet" on their
future intentions (regarding providing "conforming" implementations).

## Thread: Questions for current IBM "mainframe" COBOL users/sites — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Questions for current IBM "mainframe" COBOL users/sites

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T14:47:54-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9u3ijt$kho$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
(Sent to both IBM-MAIN and comp.lang.cobol) - Please feel free to pass this
on to anyone else who might be interested/have input but doesn't watch
either of these lists.

I am curious (and this is NOT an official - much less "scientific" survey).

Given this week's IBM announcement and what is listed in its "compatibility"
section:

1) How many existing IBM COBOL sites still have any COBOL programs compiled
(and maintained) with the CMPR2 compiler option?  When VS COBOL II V3.0
first came out (MANY years ago) this was medium common - especially to avoid
file-status=39 problems.  I think besides FS=39 issues, there are still some
ODO (Occurs Depending On) issues that some sites MIGHT still be using it
for.  Others may be using it just because "that's the way we have always
done things" - but as it never was supported with VisualAge COBOL and is now
"going away" on the mainframe, I am curious how many sites this will impact.

2) How many existing sites have any "production" (whatever YOU mean by that)
COBOL code running under VM/CMS - *not* under VSE under VM?  When I worked
for a 3rd party software tool vendor, we had about 2 sites worldwide using
COBOL under VM.  I am curious whether the fact that V3R1 won't be available
for VM will actually impact anyone.
```

#### ↳ Re: Questions for current IBM "mainframe" COBOL users/sites

- **From:** "Larry Kahm" <lkahm@bellatlantic.net>
- **Date:** 2001-11-28T22:03:59+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jbdN7.921$h24.69609@typhoon1.gnilink.net>`

```
I was contracted by a financial services firm in the New York metropolitan
area at the end of last year to perform the analysis of what it would take
to migrate from VS COBOL II to COBOL for OS/390.

Needless to say, the proposal was all that was ever done on that project.
Management felt that a gradual, rather than forced, migration would work for
their limited budget.

From what I recall, there were about 16,000 batch programs and about 9,000
online programs.  Approximately 75% of them used CMPR2.

I was astonished - but the firm processed this stuff every day and every
night...
```

#### ↳ Re: Questions for current IBM "mainframe" COBOL users/sites

NOCMPR2 one of the default compile options.

Getting people to convert from OS/VS COBOL to COBOL II was a huge

I know we've never used CMPR2.

<a id="t-a7f4e15a3d"></a>
## Thread: Need for 64 bit addressing in COBOL was Re: New COBOL announced. — 1 this month / 2 total

### Need for 64 bit addressing in COBOL was Re: New COBOL announced.

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-11-29T01:27:47+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C058F49.765D27A2@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<200111281824.fASIOKW137488@westrelay01.boulder.ibm.com>`

```
If the AIX and AS400 COBOL products support 64 bit addressing and there
are shops taking advantage of the capability, then lack of that
capability on the mainframe would be a barrier to migrating those
workloads to the z/OS environment.  If the z/OS C/C++ and Java programs
are supporting and using 64 bit addressing especially in the Websphere
environment, COBOL needs it for interoperability.  If there are COBOL
programs in other environments (DEC Alpha, SUN?, and HP-UX?) that take
advantage of 64-bit, then lack of the capability would be a hindrance to
migration to z/OS.  Windows will be supporting 64-bit with Itanium. 
Linux is supporting 64 bit in some environments.  64 bits may become
desirable for large CICS regions where the invidual transaction doesn't
need 64 bits worth of space but the concurrent transactions collectively
do.  Also handling of BLOBs (Binary Large OBjects) in CICS may force the
issue.  As COBOL starts handling non-traditional data the addressing
requirement increases because the size of the individual data aggregate
increases.  For example, your payroll record may include a picture of
the employee and their fingerprint.  Indeed we probably have to look at
what people are doing on other platforms in order to better understand
future needs.  

When data management gets its act together with a universal access
method and control block that will handle all of the currently supported
data formats (CKD sequential, ESDS, KSDS, RRDS, HFS, PDSE etc. plus
VTAM, etc.) in the 64 bit environment, COBOL will need 64 bit addressing
to take advantage of the capability. 

Tom Ross wrote:
> 
>  Does anyone in IBMMAIN know of 'real-world' (no retirees please) reqirements
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…

#### ↳ Re: Hi, Jackson Structured Programming??

- **From:** Charles <nospam@newsranger.com>

On Fri, 30 Nov 2001 12:42:55 -0000, in article
<9u7uq0$f0g$1@plutonium.btinternet.com>, spamkiller stated 
>

Give us the author and publisher of the book and maybe someone has seen or read
the book before, and can comment on it.

Some of the people in the forum have written books of their own, like learn
COBOL in 24 Hours. 

I have an old structured programming book at Home but the name Jackson does not
sound familiar.  I think mine was written by Stern and Stern.  I have seen some
other books for sale like Murach's Structured Programming.  He wrote books on
both basic COBOL and CICS.  A lot depends on if you are using a compiler
designed for the Mainframe, the AS/400, or a PC.  I tend to like books written
with lots of complete programs as examples.  When you have a complete program to
study, you can learn how the parts work and fit together.

A lot of programming is just do A, B, C, and then repeat till you get to the end
of the file.  This is a little over simplification but even complex problems can
be subdivided into smaller problems.  Structured programming is a way of
splitting up the code into sections that can be performed as needed.  The hard
part is learning to be creative and put the same old pieces together in new
ways.

>Hi all,
>        I am just starting a course. I must admit the amount of info in the
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…

>Paul.

If your library has it, you can look up chapter 9 of David Budgen's
"Software Design" (ISBN 0201544032 ). I am not recommending that you
buy it - it's pricey, (58$US at Amazon.com), if all you need are the
30 pages on JSP. That's enough of an overview for you to understand
how JSP works (and apply it), and why one would use JSP instead of
another design method.

By the way, that's what the book is about - comparing design methods.
The methods are from before 1994, the publication date, and JSP, in
case you're wondering, from the 1970s - not exactly cutting edge, but
probably useful for simple program development.

Robaire

Willem Clements wrote:

> >By the way, that's what the book is about - comparing design methods.
> >The methods are from before 1994, the publication date, and JSP, in
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…

Willem,

There is validity to Robaire's comment, "not exactly cutting edge", and also
accepting that you were around in the seventies, bright eyed and bushy tailed <G>.

In my own case there are two things which have stood the test of time - Decision
Tables, which I think Richard said were around in the fifties. My use of these was
initially in a systems analysis capacity. Their usefulness still holds true.

It wasn't called a technique per se,  but Dan McCracken's "Structured Programming"
set me on the right course. The essential thing here is that the book gave coded
examples, whereas much of the material on design approaches, waffles on in
generalities. A case in point - I have two books on OO COBOL - based obviously on
very early renditions of the proposed J4 Standard. They aren't coupled to coded
examples and where they do show code are so generalized, it requires a stretch of
the imagination to discern where their examples could be used with a 'live'
compiler.

At the current time we have "Teach Yourself COBOL in 24 Hours" and  "Teach ......
etc.....in 21 Days". The first being the better of the two, in my view. Possibly
Thane's book will see a 10 year run before it is considered 'old hat' and replaced
by something more up to date - a new offering would delve more closely into the
Internet.

Here's an interesting example, Pete unashamedly promotes RAD - absolutely nothing
wrong with that. Two points :-

- He takes a swipe at the 'waterfall' approach. About two weeks ago, with a couple
of hours to kill waiting for my wife shopping, pulled off the bookstore shelf
'RAD" by Steve McConnelll - one of the acknowledged gurus on RAD. Paraphrased
quote, "Under certain circumstances, for some applications, the waterfall approach
might be the best to follow" - I offer no comment one way or the other - but
that's from the guru.

- System design has evolved over the four decades that COBOL has existed. Pete
sensibly makes the point that an application has to have a design team, and as an
absolute MUST  include end users as active participants - agree 100%. BUT when I
started  back in '63 young 'Dashwoods' were learning how to program the damn
things and we 'elders', mainly ex bean counters, were learning to design. Then we
didn't ask the users because we were the former 'users'.

EDP has become a specialty, so it behooves us to involve users in teams. I meet a
Scottish friend when walking the dogs, invalided from Petro Canada. During his
time, roughly some five years ago - as an accountant he was an ACTIVE member of a
design team - and this sensible inclusion of end user personnel wasn't given some
fancy technical name.

I'm not knocking the various design tools that come up - but look at what each is
offering under a microscope, pick out the good stuff, possibly 10% common sense
ideas, and reject the rest as waffle.

Anybody here who is into OO who is extensively using UML or CRC cards ?

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

<a id="t-9ad3832f07"></a>
## Thread: compile process and various libraries — 2 msgs

### compile process and various libraries

- **From:** jmmx3@hotmail.com (jm)
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T09:19:00-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b6c7c1c.0111300918.4236a2f4@posting.google.com>`

```
Could someone describe what the textbook/simplified mainframe (OS/390)
cobol program compile process is,  including reference to what seems
to be standard names of srcelib,  copylib, linklib, loadlib, modlib,
proclib.

I am trying to determine if I have source code for every member name
in my loadlib, but I'm not sure if its fair to assume there should be
a "one to one" comparison.  (i.e.  for every loadlib member name,  an
equal srcelib member name)
```

#### ↳ Re: compile process and various libraries

- **From:** Steve Thompson <sthompsonNOSPAM@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T16:25:12-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C07F938.7758EFF1@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<9b6c7c1c.0111300918.4236a2f4@posting.google.com>`

```
jm wrote:
> 
> Could someone describe what the textbook/simplified mainframe (OS/390)
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> equal srcelib member name)

First off, I'm going to assume plain batch, no CICS or Data
Base processing.

1) First JOB Step is the cobol compile execution.
   Takes the source via the sysin dd statement and writes
the object to a DD (specified in the COBOL parms as I
recall). It writes the listing to SYSPRINT DD.

2) IF the prior step (generally called COB or CBL) executes
with CC -= 4, then the next step, LKED runs and takes in the
object deck from step one, matches it to the libraries in
the SYSLIB DD and produces an executable program which it
writes to SYSLMOD.

3) You now take a second existing PROC and run it to pull in
this program to test it.

As for all the rest, it depends on how your LOADLIB is
built. If all the programs that make up the LOADLIB are
written in COBOL then there should be a one for one match.
If there are user written subroutines that are statically
linked, then there will be a one (LOADLIB member) to many
(Source) match.

The DD names you have do not necessarily match the PROC
keywords.

Your SRCELIB probably is used in the SYSIN DD for the COB
step. There should also be a "MEMBER" keyword that is
appended (within parenthesis) to the SRCELIB thusly:

//SYSIN  DD DISP=SHR,DSN=&SRCELIB(&MEMBER)

Your COPYLIB is probably placed into a SYSLIB concatenation,
and there should be no member names specified.

The LINKLIB information is used by the LINKEDT (Linkage
Editor or BINDER) to pull in all the routines that COBOL
needs to match to the object that it produces (output to
SYSPUNCH or some other DD that I've forgotten). 

Your MODLIB could just be the staging library that holds all
the user provided subroutines (or object decks).

Lastly, your PROCLIB will not contain any relationship
whatsoever to the load library (as to names) as all it is
supposed to contain are JCL procedures (PROCs) that are
called from your JCL.

Example:

//COMPILE1 EXEC COBCL,SRCELIB=WWY81.PROD.SOURCE,MEMBER=PGM1,
//            MODLIB=WWY81.PROD.OBJECT,
//            LOADLIB=WWY81.PROD.LOAD 

This could expand into 20-50 JCL statements, and any keyword
you didn't specify would default to some value (look in the
PROCLIB where ever it is and see).

Now, if this was a homework assignment and not some
programmer trying to figure out an "MVS" system, I hope you
are far enough along for some of this to make sense. Because
I didn't say anything about your copylib which you should be
able to figure out for yourself in any case.
```

---

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>

```
Before you get (too many) nasty replies, it sounds like you are at the VERY
beginning of learning about COBOL. (The terms that you mention are usually
covered within the first few weeks of most COBOL classes.)

Can you tell us HOW you are learning COBOL (or why you want this
information) and what you have done so far to find out about this yourself?
(Class? text-book? lectures? other?)

By the way, I will start you with one "hint" COMPUTATIONAL (or COMP or
COMP-n) is VERY dependent upon which COBOL compiler and upon which operating
system you are working.  If you reply to this note (in the newsgroup) - you
probably should tell us that as well.
```

<a id="t-e061057fef"></a>
## Thread: Cobol: Structured vs. "Drop-Down" Performance — 2 this month / 29 total

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Cobol: Structured vs. "Drop-Down" Performance

- **From:** j6vflbl6vy6g8o001@sneakemail.com (Kevin Arthur)
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T19:11:46-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e87297f1.0111301911.1e4a08f4@posting.google.com>`

```
Without getting into a major philosophical debate on the use of "goto"
vs. structured programming, can someone just quantify for me the
(possible) PERFORMANCE difference between the two methodologies?

I understand (and read the threads) on the code maintenance issues of
(so-called) structured programming vs. (so-called) un-structured
programming.

Please assume that maintenance is not an issue (which of course it
really is), and that I am solely concerned with raw performance. 
Here's why: The legacy of Cobol coding at the company where I work has
been that "drop down" programming is faster than structured
programming (i.e. using "perform" to execute modular paragraphs).

In other words, logic starts at the beginning of the procedure
division and just goes straight down, with an occasional goto loop or
inline perform until it gets to the end.  In practical terms you can't
entirely get rid of some procedures, so resulting program generally
ends up something like this:

Procedure Division
- Initialization Logic (in-line)
- XXX-Main-Loop
- Read Next Record 
      AT END GO TO Termination-Logic
- Main Logic Body (in-line)
- GO TO XXX-Main-Loop
- Support Paragraphs
- Termination-Logic (in-line)


I recently re-wrote in structured format a program like the above. 
Over time it had simply become an un-maintainable abomination.

My boss and a co-worker insist however, that the new program simply
must be suffering from a performance hit as a result.  My boss is not
concerned with maintenance; these are programs used for conversions,
so he considers them one-time programs.  (Major changes are still done
for new conversions, however, hence my re-write)

So, down on the bare metal, performance-wise:

A "PERFORM" causes registers to be pushed onto a stack, a jump
instruction to be executed, registers to be popped from a stack, and a
return jump instruction to be executed on each iteration (at the very
least).  But, an optimizing compiler can optimize the code because it
can "see" what the code is doing, no?

A Microfocus document that came with their (old) 2.5.39 compiler even
stated:

"Put commonly used pieces of code in sections or paragraphs and
PERFORM them. This is saves space for any statement used more than
once that produces more than 4 bytes of generated code (in a NOTRICKLE
program).  It is often beneficial even on single statements, for
example edited moves, subprogram calls or file I/O."

Does this still apply?  No equivalent document in later versions.


A "GO TO" does not push/pop a stack.  It does, of course, execute
going and returning jump instructions.  However, can a compiler
optimize code with 'goto's?  (Of course, effect depends on how many or
few there are)


What is the effect of either method on the "fetch-decode-execute" flow
and processor cache loading of a modern micro-processor?  It was my
understanding that "goto"s break the flow and cause the processor to
have to flush (some part of) the instruction cache in order to start
reading at the new location where the goto's going, thus wasting the
read-ahead load of the instruction cache.

How does an optimizing compiler (e.g. Microfocus if it can be termed
that) handle a "perform" in this sense?

Obviously I'm not an expert in this area.  Any and all comments and
suggestions will be greatly appreciated!

Thank you much!

Kevin Arthur
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol: Structured vs. "Drop-Down" Performance

- **From:** Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-11-30T23:10:11-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3C086632.75BD6D9F@mb.sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<e87297f1.0111301911.1e4a08f4@posting.google.com>`

```
Kevin Arthur wrote:

Just replying to one bit of your post:

>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

So does any other instruction that breaks the program flow  If you perform
some paragraph that's way off in the other end of the program, it is
highly unlikely that it'll be in the cache.  Or if you're in a big perform
loop, you may well incur the same penalty when you go from the bottom to
the top of the perform.  (Depends on the size of the cache, of course, and
how many programs are executing concurrently).  Whether or not goto's are
explicitly used in the source, the object code has to use them, so the
point is moot.

PL
```
